The Five Universals
advanced
10 min
Inclusion of Hypothetical Universals in the Threefold Division
Defense of including hypothetical universals in the threefold division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why hypothetical universals (kulliyyat fardiyya) can be included in the threefold division, and refute the objection that assuming them true in reality leads to impossibility.

Core Idea
Hypothetical universals are included via possibility of assumption, not actuality.

The commentator addresses an important question: Are hypothetical universals (those that could be true of many but are not actually) included in the threefold division? He argues they are, because the division is based on the possibility of being assumed true of many, even if they have no instances in reality. He refutes an objection that such assumption is impossible, showing that the impossibility of actual truth does not preclude the possibility of assumption. This lesson clarifies the scope of the threefold division and its application to concepts like 'necessary being' in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about hypothetical universals
The commentator states: 'It is apparent that hypothetical universals are included in these three divisions.' Find this phrase and note that he asserts their inclusion.
The Arabic: 'ثم الظاهر أن الكليات الفرضية داخلة في هذه الأقسام الثلاثة'.
2
Understand the ground for inclusion
The reason given is the possibility of assuming them true of many (إمكان فرض صدقها على كثيرين) based on a mere concept that necessitates the possibility of assuming the three divisions in them.
Even if no actual instances exist, the concept can be considered as having possible instances.
3
Refute the objection
An objector says assuming them true in reality is impossible (محال). The commentator responds that the impossibility of actual truth does not make the assumption impossible itself. In fact, both the assumption and the assumed could be impossible, but that does not prevent the universals from being true of them under the assumption.
He uses the example of five universals being true of a single thing under assumption, which is impossible in reality but possible in assumption.
4
Consider the alternative exclusion
The commentator notes that hypothetical universals could be excluded if we consider the relation to what they are predicated on in reality. But he ultimately accepts inclusion, following the pattern of including the concept of the necessary (wajib) in definitions.
This shows that even concepts with no actual instances (like necessary being) are treated as having possible particulars.
5
Apply to kalam example
In kalam, the concept of 'necessary being' (wajib al-wujud) is a hypothetical universal because there is only one instance (Allah). Yet, the threefold division applies to it when considered relative to the possibility of assuming many necessary beings (even if impossible in reality). This helps in defining attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل الأقسام الثلاثة، ثم قيد بكونه من الجزئيات؛ سواء كانت من تبعيضية أو ابتدائية؛ أي: حال كونه بعضا منها أو ناشئا منها؛ للإشارة إلى أن المعتبر النسبة إلى جزئي واحد؛ أي جزئي كان؛ لا إلى مجموع الجزئيات؛ لأنه يبطل الحصر، إذ هنا أقسام أربعة أخر؛ هي أن تجتمع في الكلي تلك الأقسام الثلاثة ثناء أو ثلاث، ولا إلى جزئي واحد معين؛ لأنه حينئذ تصير الأقسام متباينة، وقد اعتبر تصادقها، حيث ذكر الجنس في تمام الماهية وجزئيتها، بل هو معتبر على إطلاقه، فتكون الأقسام متخالفة بالاعتبار على ما صرحوا به من جواز اجتماع الخمسة في كلي واحد، ثم الجزئي الواحد لا يجوز أن يراد به الحقيقي، وإلا أخرج الأجناس والفصول العالية والمتوسطة وخواصها وأعراضها مقيسة إلى الماهية التي هي أجناس متوسطة أو سافلة، الإضافي، وللإشارة إلى ذلك عبر عنه بقوله: ما تحته هذا؛ لكن يرد الناطق مقيسا إلى الحيوان؛ فإنه خاصة له مع عدم دخوله في الكلي المنسوب إلى ما تحته من جزئياته، إلا أن يقال: ما يحمل عليه شيء فهو جزئي إضافي له، ثم الظاهر أن الكليات الفرضية داخلة في هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، وذلك لأن إمكان فرض صدقها على كثيرين؛ نظرا إلى مجرد مفهوم يستدعي إمكان فرض الأقسام الثلاثة فيها، وإن لم يكن شيء منها في نفس الأمر، فاندفع ما قيل: إن فرض صدقها في نفس الأمر محال، فيجوز أن يستلزم المحال بأن لا يكون شيء من الأقسام الثلاثة، وأنه يجوز فرض صدقها نفسا وجزءا وخارجا بالنسبة إلى أمر واحد فيلزم صدق الكليات الخمس عليها بالنسبة إلى ذلك الأمر؛ لأن الفرض والمفروض كلاهما ممتنعان، إذ لا يمكن للعقل تجويز كونها نفسا وجزءا وخارجا بالنسبة إلى أمر واحد، ويجوز أن تخرج الكليات الفرضية، وتعتبر النسبة إلى ما يحمل عليه في نفس الأمر؛ بناء على عدم تعلق الفرض الحكمي بأحوال الكليات الفرضية، ويكون إدخالها في التعريف تبع إدخال مفهوم الواجب فيه، وهذا على طبق ما قالوا في النسب بين الكليات؛ فإن بعضهم
Rather, the three divisions, then he restricted it to being from the particulars; whether they are from partitioning or original; i.e., being a part of them or arising from them; to indicate that what is considered is the relation to a single particular, any particular whatsoever; not to the set of particulars, because then the division would be invalid, for here there are four other divisions; that those three divisions combine in the universal two or three times, nor to a single particular specifically, because then the divisions would be disparate, whereas their coincidence was considered, where genus was mentioned in the entirety of the quiddity and its partiality, but rather it is considered absolutely, so the divisions differ by consideration as they explicitly stated about the permissibility of the five coming together in one universal. Then the single particular cannot be intended as the real particular; otherwise the higher and intermediate genera and their properties and accidents measured to the quiddity that is intermediate or lower genera would be excluded, the relative [particular]. And to indicate that he expressed it by his statement: 'what is under this'; but the objection arises concerning 'rational' measured to 'animal', for it is a property of it without being included in the universal related to what is under it of its particulars, unless it is said: whatever something is predicated on is a relative particular to it. Then it is apparent that the hypothetical universals are included in these three divisions, because the possibility of assuming them true of many, with respect to a mere concept that necessitates the possibility of assuming the three divisions in them, even if none of them exist in reality. So what was said is refuted: that assuming them true in reality is impossible, so it is permissible that the impossible implies that none of the three divisions exist, and that it is permissible to assume them true as essence, part, and external relative to one thing, then the five universals would be true of it relative to that thing; because both the assumption and the assumed are impossible, since the intellect cannot countenance their being essence, part, and external relative to one thing. And it is permissible that the hypothetical universals be excluded, and the relation is considered to what they are predicated on in reality, based on the lack of attachment of the judicial assumption to the states of hypothetical universals, and their inclusion in the definition follows the inclusion of the concept of the necessary in it. And this is in accordance with what they said about the relations among universals; for some of them
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the definition of the threefold division of universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) and the condition of being 'from the particulars'. The commentator explains that the three divisions (being essential, part of the quiddity, and external) are considered relative to a single particular, not to a set of particulars or a specific particular. He defends this by showing that if it were to a set, there would be four additional divisions (combinations), and if to a specific particular, the divisions would be separate whereas they are supposed to coincide. He then addresses the issue of relative particulars (juz'i idafi) and hypothetical universals (kulliyyat fardiyya). He argues that hypothetical universals, even if they cannot actually be true of many, can be assumed to be true, and thus they are included in the three divisions. He refutes an objection that assuming them true in reality is impossible, explaining that the assumption itself is impossible, but that does not prevent them from being considered under the three divisions. He concludes by noting that the inclusion of hypothetical universals in the definition follows the pattern of including the concept of the necessary (wajib) in definitions. This passage uses examples like 'animal' as a genus and 'rational' as a property relative to 'animal' to illustrate relative particularity.

Key Terms
كلي فرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the basis for including hypothetical universals in the threefold division?
A) They are actually true of many in reality.
B) They can be assumed to be true of many.
C) They are included only if they have at least one real particular.
D) The commentator excludes them.

Key Takeaway
Hypothetical universals are included in the threefold division because the division relies on the possibility of assumption, not actual existence, allowing even unique concepts like 'necessary being' to be analyzed logically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the concept of 'angel' in Islamic theology. Angels are a species with many individuals. However, the concept of 'Jibril' (a specific angel) is a particular. Using the lesson's reasoning, explain whether the threefold division applies to 'Jibril' as a hypothetical universal when considered as a species (even though there is only one Jibril).
?
In a discussion about the attributes of Allah, someone argues that 'knowledge' cannot be a universal because it only applies to Allah. Refute this using the commentator's argument about hypothetical universals.

What is Genus? The Predication of Agreement
Author defines genus and specifies predication type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct type of predication (haml al-muwata'a) in the definition of genus and distinguish it from derivation and composition predications.

Core Idea
Genus is predicated with unitive agreement, not derivation or composition.

This lesson begins the five universals with genus. The author's definition is simple: 'Genus is that which is predicated.' But the commentator immediately clarifies: this predication must be of the agreement type (haml muwata'a). Understanding this nuance is crucial because it determines how we classify things under genus in Islamic logic and sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the Arabic 'الجنس: وهو المقول'. What does 'al-maqool' mean literally?
2
Identify the type of predication
The commentator specifies 'المحمول حمل مواطأة'. What is haml al-muwata'a? How is it different from 'Zaid is a striker' or 'Zaid is possessor of wealth'?
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the statement 'The human is an animal'. Is this predication of agreement? Why? (Because 'human' and 'animal' are one in reality, human is an animal in essence.)
4
Contrast with derivation and composition
Take 'Zaid is a writer' (derivation) and 'Zaid is possessor of wealth' (composition). How do they differ from agreement?
5
Summarise why only haml al-muwata'a is considered
Universals like genus are predicated on many things univocally, sharing the same essence, not through derivation or composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مبادئ التصورات [الجنس] الأول: الجنس: وهو المقول (قوله: وهو المقول) أي: المحمول حمل مواطأة، وهو حمل هو هو كأن يقال: زيد قائم، فيحكم عليه بالتغاير؛ بحسب الذهن، والاتحاد؛ بحسب الخارج؛ لأن المعتبر في كلية الكلي مطلقا؛ جنسا كان أو غيره، هذا الحمل دون حمل الاشتقاق، وهو حمل المبدأ بواسطة حمل المشتق، تحمل الضرب على زيد في "زيد ضارب"، وإفادة قيامه به بواسطة حمل الضارب عليه، ودون حمل التركيب، وهو حمل ذو هو تحمل المال على زيد في «زيد ذو مال»، وإفادة تعلقه به بواسطة حمل هذا التركيب عليه. وقوله: (وهو المقول)؛ أي: المحمول؛ أي: الصالح للمقولية، وهذا التعريف رسم، وإنما كان رسما؛ لأن الكلي وإن كان جنسا، لكن المقول على كثيرين أمر عارض له غير مقوم له، وإنما ذكر ليتعلق به لفظ على كذا، أو في جواب كذا. العطار يخصها بما سوى الأمور الشاملة ونقائضها، وبعضهم عممها؛ قاله عبد الحكيم، وإنما نقلت عبارته برمتها لأمرين؛ الأول: لعموم فائدتها، والثاني: للتنبيه على ما وقع في بعض الحواشي هنا؛ فإنه أخذها وفرقها في مواضع؛ ساكتا عن العزو. (قوله: الأول الجنس) هو لفظ عربي، وهو الضرب، وهو أعم من النوع على ما في الصحاح، وما أوهمه كلام شرح المطالع من أنه يوناني؛ غير مطابق للواقع. (قوله: وهو المقول) أي: المحمول حمل مواطأة؛ لأنه المعتبر في باب الكليات، كما هو حقيقته عند الشيخ، وفي الأساس إنه مشترك بين حمل هو هو وحمل ذو هو الشامل لحمل التركيب وحمل الاشتقاق، ولما اختلف في أن هذه التعريفات حدود أو رسوم، وترجيح أحد الجانبين؛ لا يتبين إلا بمعرفة أن...
Chapter on the principles of concepts. [Genus] The first: genus: and it is that which is predicated. (His statement: 'and it is that which is predicated') meaning: the predicate with predication of agreement, which is predication of 'is is', such as saying 'Zaid is standing', so it is judged that they differ with respect to the mind, and unite with respect to external reality; because what is considered in the universality of the universal absolutely, whether it is genus or other, is this predication, not the predication of derivation, which is the predication of the derivative by means of the derived term, e.g., the predication of 'striking' on Zaid in 'Zaid is a striker', indicating that he performs it by means of predicating 'striker' on him, nor the predication of composition, which is the predication of 'possessor of', e.g., predication of 'wealth' on Zaid in 'Zaid is possessor of wealth', indicating his connection to it by means of predicating this compound on him. And his statement: 'and it is that which is predicated' means: the predicate, i.e., that which is suitable for predication. And this definition is a description (rasm), and it is only a description because the universal, even if it is genus, but 'predicated on many' is an accidental attribute of it not constitutive of it, and it is only mentioned so that the expression 'on' or 'in answer to' is attached to it. Al-Attar: he restricts it to what is other than the comprehensive things and their opposites, and some generalized it; said by Abd al-Hakim. I only transmitted his statement in its entirety for two reasons: first, for its general benefit, and second, to alert to what occurred in some glosses here; for he took it and scattered it in places, silent about attribution. (His statement: 'the first: genus') is an Arabic word, meaning 'type', and it is more general than 'species' according to what is in al-Sihah. What the wording of Sharh al-Mutali' suggests, that it is Greek, is not in accordance with reality. (His statement: 'and it is that which is predicated') meaning: the predicate with predication of agreement; because it is the one considered in the chapter of universals, as is its reality according to al-Shaykh. And in al-Asas it is ambiguous between the predication of 'is is' and the predication of 'possessor of', which includes the predication of composition and derivation. And since there is disagreement about whether these definitions are essential definitions (hudud) or descriptions (rusum), and preferring one side, it is not clear except by knowing that...
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of genus (jins), which is the first of the five universals. The author first quotes the matn: 'The first: genus: and it is that which is predicated (al-maqool).' Then the commentator expands on the meaning of 'al-maqool', clarifying that it means 'predicated with predication of agreement (haml al-muwata'a)'. He distinguishes three types of predication: (1) predication of agreement (haml muwata'a), where the subject and predicate are one in essence but differ in concept, e.g., 'Zaid is standing' (Zaid and standing are one in reality but different in the mind); (2) predication of derivation (haml al-ishtiqaq), e.g., 'Zaid is a striker' (the derivative 'striker' is predicated, implying the derived meaning 'striking'); (3) predication of composition (haml al-tarkib), e.g., 'Zaid is possessor of wealth' (a compound predicate). The commentator emphasizes that in defining universals, only predication of agreement is considered. He then states that this definition of genus is a rasm (description), not a hadd (essential definition), because 'predicated on many' is an accidental attribute of genus, not part of its essence. The definition is provided to attach expressions like 'on many' or 'in answer to what it is' to the genus. The commentator also mentions that al-Attar restricts the definition to exclude comprehensive things and their opposites, while others generalize it. He explains that he quoted al-Attar's statement in full for its benefit and to point out plagiarism in some glosses. Additionally, he notes that the term 'jins' is Arabic, meaning 'type', more general than 'naw' (species), and that the claim it is Greek is false. Finally, he remarks that there is disagreement about whether these definitions are hudud or rusum, but resolving this requires knowing the nature of definitions.

Key Terms
الجنس
المقول
حمل المواطأة
حمل الاشتقاق
حمل التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which type of predication is considered in the definition of genus?
A) Predication of derivation (haml al-ishtiqaq)
B) Predication of composition (haml al-tarkib)
C) Predication of agreement (haml al-muwata'a)
D) All three are equally valid

Key Takeaway
Genus is defined using predication of agreement, where subject and predicate share the same reality but differ in concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement 'The human is a body'. Is this an example of predication of agreement? Why or why not? Compare it with 'The human is a writer' and 'The human is possessor of knowledge'.
?
In usul al-fiqh, the term 'hukm' (ruling) is defined as 'a speech of the Lawgiver regarding the actions of the responsible person'. Identify the genus in this definition and explain whether the predication is of agreement, derivation, or composition.

5 min
Is the Definition of Genus a Hadd or a Rasm?
Author clarifies that the definition of genus is a rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition of genus as 'that which is predicated on many' is a rasm (description) and not a hadd (essential definition).

Core Idea
The definition of genus is a rasm because 'predicated on many' is an accidental attribute, not part of its essence.

In the previous lesson, we learned the definition of genus. But is this definition a true essential definition (hadd) or merely a description (rasm)? The commentator addresses this directly, stating that the definition is a rasm because the property 'predicated on many' is a non-constitutive accident. In this lesson, we examine why and what that means for the logical classification of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the claim
Find where the commentator says 'وهذا التعريف رسم'. This is the explicit statement that the definition is a rasm.
2
Understand the reason
The commentator says: 'لأن الكلي وإن كان جنسا، لكن المقول على كثيرين أمر عارض له غير مقوم له'. 'عارض' means accidental, 'غير مقوم' means non-constitutive. So 'predicated on many' is not part of the essence of genus.
3
Contrast hadd vs rasm
Hadd gives the essence (quiddity) of a thing; rasm gives an accidental property that distinguishes it. For genus, the essence is being predicated on many differing in quiddity. 'Predicated on many' is a property that follows from that essence.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the definition of 'i'tiqad' (belief) as 'assent to something'. In usul al-fiqh, some define it as 'assent with certainty'. Is that a hadd or rasm? (Assent is essential, certainty may be an accident.)
5
Conclude
Knowing whether a definition is hadd or rasm affects its reliability in argumentation. A hadd is more precise and essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a commentary on the definition of genus (jins), which is the first of the five universals. The author first quotes the matn: 'The first: genus: and it is that which is predicated (al-maqool).' Then the commentator expands on the meaning of 'al-maqool', clarifying that it means 'predicated with predication of agreement (haml al-muwata'a)'. He distinguishes three types of predication: (1) predication of agreement (haml muwata'a), where the subject and predicate are one in essence but differ in concept, e.g., 'Zaid is standing' (Zaid and standing are one in reality but different in the mind); (2) predication of derivation (haml al-ishtiqaq), e.g., 'Zaid is a striker' (the derivative 'striker' is predicated, implying the derived meaning 'striking'); (3) predication of composition (haml al-tarkib), e.g., 'Zaid is possessor of wealth' (a compound predicate). The commentator emphasizes that in defining universals, only predication of agreement is considered. He then states that this definition of genus is a rasm (description), not a hadd (essential definition), because 'predicated on many' is an accidental attribute of genus, not part of its essence. The definition is provided to attach expressions like 'on many' or 'in answer to what it is' to the genus. The commentator also mentions that al-Attar restricts the definition to exclude comprehensive things and their opposites, while others generalize it. He explains that he quoted al-Attar's statement in full for its benefit and to point out plagiarism in some glosses. Additionally, he notes that the term 'jins' is Arabic, meaning 'type', more general than 'naw' (species), and that the claim it is Greek is false. Finally, he remarks that there is disagreement about whether these definitions are hudud or rusum, but resolving this requires knowing the nature of definitions.

Why Genus Comes First in Definitions
Author explains why genus precedes other universals and defends omitting 'kulli' from definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dependency relationship that requires genus to precede the differentia and relative species, and apply this principle to a definition from an Islamic science.

Core Idea
To define the differentia, you must first know the genus.

In this passage, the author explains why genus must be introduced before the differentia and the relative species. This is not arbitrary; it is because understanding the differentia requires knowing what genus it distinguishes within. Similarly, the relative species is defined by its genus. This principle is essential for constructing correct definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claims of priority
The author states genus is needed for the differentia and for the relative species. Locate the Arabic phrases: وَعَلَى الْفَصْلِ لِاحْتِيَاجِنَا... and وَعَلَى النَّوْعِ لِتَوَقُّفِ مَعْرِفَةِ...
2
Understand the justification for differentia
Why does the differentia need genus? Because proximate differentia (e.g., rational) distinguishes within a proximate genus; remote differentia (e.g., sensitive) distinguishes within a remote genus. Without the genus, you cannot specify what the differentia is distinguishing.
The passage gives examples: 'natiq' (rational) and 'hassas' (sensitive).
3
Understand the justification for relative species
The relative species (e.g., animal considered as a species of living body) is defined by taking its genus. So knowing the genus is prior to knowing the species.
Al-Dasuqi explains that what is needed must be presented before that which needs it.
4
Apply this to an Islamic definition
Consider the definition of 'hadd' (fixed punishment) in fiqh. The genus is 'disciplinary measure' and the differentia is 'specified by shari'a'. Could you define it without first knowing the genus?

الصَّالِحُ لِلْمَقُولِيَّةِ بِحَسَبِ نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ أَيْ لَا بِحَسَبِ الْفَرْضِ وَهُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنَ الْكُلِّيِّ وَيَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِ الْأَخَصِّ وُجُودُ الْأَعَمِّ. وَذَكَرَ الْمُصَنِّفُ فِي تَفْصِيلًا. لَا يُقَالُ مَفْهُومُ الْكُلِّيِّ هُوَ الصَّالِحُ لِأَنْ يُقَالَ بِالْفَرْضِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ وَمَفْهُومُ الْمَقُولِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مَا كَانَ مَقُولًا عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ بِالْفِعْلِ فَلَا يُغْنِي عَنْهُ لِأَنَّ دَلَالَةَ الْمَقُولِ بِالْفِعْلِ عَلَى الصَّالِحِ لِأَنْ يُقَالَ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ الْتِزَامٌ وَدَلَالَةُ الِالْتِزَامِ لَيْسَتْ مُعْتَبَرَةً فِي التَّعْرِيفَاتِ لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ لَمْ يُرَدْ بِالْمَقُولِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ إِلَّا الصَّالِحُ لِأَنْ يُقَالَ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ إِذْ لَوْ أُرِيدَ بِهِ الْمَقُولُ بِالْفِعْلِ يَخْرُجُ عَنْ تَعْرِيفِ الْمَقُولُ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ بِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّيِّ. وَأَمَّا مَا أَوْرَدَهُ عَلَيْهِ الدَّوَّانِيُّ أَوَّلًا بِأَنَّ الْكُلِّيَّ هُوَ الَّذِي يُمْكِنُ فَرْضُ الشَّرِكَةِ فِيهِ أَيْ فَرْضُ مَقُولِيَّتِهِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ وَلَوْ حُمِلَ الْمَقُولُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ عَلَى مَا يُمْكِنُ فَرْضُ مَقُولِيَّتِهِ لَدَخَلَ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ الْكُلِّيَّاتُ الْفَرْضِيَّةُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْحَقَائِقِ الْمَوْجُودَةِ إِذْ يُمْكِنُ فَرْضُ مَقُولِيَّتِهَا عَلَيْهَا بَلِ الْكُلِّيَّاتُ الْمُتَبَايِنَةُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْمَاهِيَّةِ مُطْلَقًا وَأَمَّا ثَانِيًا فَلِأَنَّ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ الَّتِي لَيْسَتْ لَهَا أَفْرَادٌ أَصْلًا لَيْسَتْ أَجْنَاسًا لِشَيْءٍ فَلَا بَأْسَ بِخُرُوجِهَا وَمِنْ هَهُنَا يَنْقَدِحُ أَنَّ الْمُنْحَصِرَ فِي الْخَمْسِ هُوَ الْكُلِّيَّاتُ الَّتِي لَهَا أَفْرَادٌ
And upon the differentia, because of our need in knowing the proximate and remote differentia for the genus. And upon the species, because the knowledge of a part of the species, which is the relative species, depends on the genus. And he omitted from the definition of genus and the rest of the universals the word 'universal' because 'said of many' suffices for it. Al-Dasuqi said: His statement 'because of our need' etc. means that what is needed must be presented before that which needs it. His statement 'the proximate differentia' like 'rational', which is what distinguishes from the partner in the proximate genus, and 'the remote' which is what distinguishes from the partner in the remote genus, like 'sensitive'. His statement 'because the knowledge of' etc. And that is because he took the genus in the definition of the relative species as will come. Al-'Attar said: And since what is said and predicated is only the universal, not the particular, and such as 'Zayd' is interpreted by this named 'Zayd'. And it was said: Because 'said' means that which is suitable for predication according to actual reality, not according to supposition, and it is more specific than the universal, and from the existence of the more specific follows the existence of the more general. And the author mentioned in the commentary of al-Talkhis that what is said and predicated is only the universal, not the particular. And similarly in the gloss of al-Jalal, for he said: The omission of the word 'universal' is not because the phrase 'said of many' suffices for it, since the universal is its genus, and mentioning the genus is obligatory in complete definitions. The truth is what the commentator said, and it is supported by the statement of al-Sayyid: The concept of 'universal' is the very concept of 'said of many', except that the word 'universal' indicates it summarily, and the phrase 'said of many' indicates it in detail. It cannot be said: The concept of 'universal' is that which is suitable to be said by supposition of many, and the concept of 'said of many' is what is actually said of many, so it does not suffice for it, because the indication of what is actually said upon what is suitable to be said of many is implicational, and implicational indication is not considered in definitions; because we say: By 'said of many' in the definition of the universals nothing but what is suitable to be said of many is meant, since if what is actually said were meant, there would exit from the definition of the universals universal concepts that have no individuals existing in the external or in the mind, whether they have no individuals at all like the hypothetical universals, or they have one individual in the external and mind based on the proof of the impossibility of the multiplicity of the Necessary in external and mind, for they are not actually said, but only by suitability; thus 'said of many' means the universal. As for what al-Dawwani objected against it first: that the universal is that in which the sharing can be supposed, i.e., the supposition of its being said of many; if 'said' in the definition is taken as that whose being said can be supposed, then the hypothetical universals would enter the definition in relation to existing realities, since their being said upon them can be supposed; indeed the mutually exclusive universals in relation to the quiddity absolutely. And second: because the universals that have no individuals at all are not genera for anything, so there is no harm in their exclusion. From this it becomes evident that what is confined to the five are the universals that have individuals.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the priority of genus over other universals and the omission of the term 'universal' (kulli) from their definitions. The author begins by stating that genus is presented before the differentia (fasl) because we need genus to know both the proximate differentia (e.g., 'rational' which distinguishes humans from other animals) and the remote differentia (e.g., 'sensitive' which distinguishes animals from plants). Similarly, genus precedes species (naw') because a part of the species—the relative species (al-naw' al-idafi), such as 'animal' considered as a species relative to 'living body' which is its genus—depends on the genus. The author then notes that the matn omitted the word 'al-kulli' (the universal) from the definitions of genus and the other four universals, arguing that the phrase 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' (said of many) suffices and renders 'kulli' redundant. Al-Dasuqi's commentary expands on why genus must be introduced first: it is a principle needed for understanding other logical concepts. Al-'Attar then discusses a nuance: predication applies only to universals, not particulars; even statements like 'Zayd is rational' involve a universal concept (human) applied to Zayd. A question arises: is 'maqul' (said) equivalent to 'kulli'? Some argue that 'maqul' means actually predicated, while 'kulli' means potentially predicable. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani holds that they are identical in concept but differ in expression—'kulli' is summary, 'maqul' is detailed. The passage refutes an objection: if 'maqul' meant actual predication, then universals with no actual individuals (like hypothetical universals) would be excluded from definitions, but these are still usable in logic. Ultimately, the correct view is that 'maqul' in the definitions is understood as 'suitable for predication', making it synonymous with 'kulli'. Al-Dawwani's objection that potential predication admits hypothetical universals is countered: those without individuals are not genera anyway. The lesson thus clarifies the exact relationship between 'kulli' and 'maqul' and justifies the economy of the matn.

الفصل
النوع الإضافي

Key Takeaway
Genus is the foundation for defining both the differentia and the relative species; it must be introduced first in the logical order.

Why 'Kulli' is Not Needed: The Sufficiency of 'Maqul 'ala al-Kathra'
Author explains why genus precedes other universals and defends omitting 'kulli' from definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue why the matn omits 'kulli' from the definitions of the five universals, and evaluate the main objections and responses regarding the equivalence of 'kulli' and 'maqul 'ala al-kathra'.

Core Idea
The universal and 'said of many' are conceptually identical, just expressed differently.

The matn omits the word 'al-kulli' from the definitions of the five universals, claiming that the phrase 'al-maqul 'ala al-kathra' suffices. This sparked a debate among commentators: are they synonymous? The passage presents the arguments and clarifies that 'maqul' in definitions means 'suitable for predication', making it equivalent to 'kulli'. Understanding this nuance is crucial for precise definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's claim
The matn omitted 'kulli' because 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' is enough. Find the Arabic: 'وَتَرَكَ مِنْ تَعْرِيفِ الْجِنْسِ وَسَائِرِ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ لَفْظَ الْكُلِّيِّ لِأَنَّ الْمَقُولَ عَلَى الْكَثْرَةِ مُغْنٍ عَنْهُ'.
2
Understand al-Sayyid's equivalence
Al-Sayyid al-Sharif says the concepts are identical; 'kulli' indicates summarily, 'maqul' in detail. The passage states: 'مفهوم الكلي هو مفهوم المقول على كثيرين بعينه إلا أن لفظ الكلي يدل عليه إجمالا ولفظ المقول يدل عليه تفصيلا'.
3
Refute the objection about actual vs. potential predication
If 'maqul' meant actually said, then universals with no individuals would be excluded. But 'maqul' is here intended as suitable for predication. The passage argues: 'لم يرد بالمقول على كثيرين في تعريف الكليات إلا الصالح لأن يقال على كثيرين'.
This is known from the hypothetical universals and the case of the Necessary Being.
4
Evaluate al-Dawwani's objection
Al-Dawwani worried that 'maqul' as potential predication would include hypothetical universals. The rebuttal is that those without individuals are not genera for anything, so their exclusion is fine.
The passage concludes: 'الكليات التي ليس لها أفراد أصلا ليست أجناسا لشيء فلا بأس بخروجها'.
5
Conclude why the omission is justified
The definition remains complete and correct because 'maqul' is understood as suitable for predication, making it synonymous with 'kulli'.

وَعَلَى الْفَصْلِ لِاحْتِيَاجِنَا فِي مَعْرِفَةِ الْفَصْلِ الْقَرِيبِ وَالْبَعِيدِ إِلَى الْجِنْسِ. وَعَلَى النَّوْعِ لِتَوَقُّفِ مَعْرِفَةِ قِسْمٍ مِنَ النَّوْعِ، وَهُوَ النَّوْعُ الْإِضَافِيُّ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ. وَتَرَكَ مِنْ تَعْرِيفِ الْجِنْسِ وَسَائِرِ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ لَفْظَ «الْكُلِّيِّ» لِأَنَّ الْمَقُولَ عَلَى الْكَثْرَةِ مُغْنٍ عَنْهُ. قَالَ الدَّسُوقِيُّ: قَوْلُهُ لِاخْتِيَاجِنَا إِلَخْ أَي وَالْمُحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ يَجِبُ تَقْدِيمُهُ عَلَى الْمُحْتَاجِ. قَوْلُهُ الْفَصْلِ الْقَرِيبِ كَنَاطِقٍ وَهُوَ مَا مَيَّزَ عَنِ الْمُشَارِكِ فِي الْجِنْسِ الْقَرِيبِ وَالْبَعِيدِ وَهُوَ مَا مَيَّزَ عَنِ الْمُشَارِكِ فِي الْجِنْسِ الْبَعِيدِ كَحَسَّاسٍ. قَوْلُهُ لِتَوَقُّفِ مَعْرِفَةِ إِلَخْ وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ أَخَذَ الْجِنْسَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ النَّوْعِ الْإِضَافِيِّ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي. قَالَ الْعَطَّارُ: وَحَيْثُ كَانَ الَّذِي يُقَالُ وَيُحْمَلُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الْكُلِّيُّ لَا الْجُزْئِيُّ، وَنَحْوُ هَذَا زَيْدٌ مُوَوَّلٌ بِهَذَا مُسَمَّى بِزَيْدٍ. وَقِيلَ لِأَنَّ الْمَقُولَ مَعْنَاهُ الصَّالِحُ لِلْمَقُولِيَّةِ بِحَسَبِ نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ أَيْ لَا بِحَسَبِ الْفَرْضِ وَهُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنَ الْكُلِّيِّ وَيَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُجُودِ الْأَخَصِّ وُجُودُ الْأَعَمِّ. وَذَكَرَ الْمُصَنِّفُ فِي شَرْحِ التَّلْخِيصِ أَنَّ الَّذِي يُقَالُ وَيُحْمَلُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الْكُلِّيُّ لَا الْجُزْئِيُّ. وَمَثَلُهُ فِي حَاشِيَةِ الْجَلَالِ فَإِنَّهُ قَالَ حَذْفُ لَفْظِ الْكُلِّيِّ لَا لِإِغْنَاءِ لَفْظِ الْمَقُولِ عَلَى الْكَثْرَةِ عَنْهُ إِذِ الْكُلِّيُّ جِنْسٌ لَهُ وَذِكْرُ الْجِنْسِ وَاجِبٌ فِي التَّعْرِيفَاتِ التَّامَّةِ. وَالْحَقُّ مَا قَالَهُ الشَّارِحُ وَيُؤَيِّدُهُ قَوْلُ السَّيِّدِ إِنَّ مَفْهُومَ الْكُلِّيِّ هُوَ مَفْهُومُ الْمَقُولِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ بِعَيْنِهِ إِلَّا أَنَّ لَفْظَ الْكُلِّيِّ يَدُلُّ عَلَيْهِ إِجْمَالًا وَلَفْظَ الْمَقُولِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ يَدُلُّ عَلَيْهِ تَفْصِيلًا. لَا يُقَالُ مَفْهُومُ الْكُلِّيِّ هُوَ الصَّالِحُ لِأَنْ يُقَالَ بِالْفَرْضِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ وَمَفْهُومُ الْمَقُولِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مَا كَانَ مَقُولًا عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ بِالْفِعْلِ فَلَا يُغْنِي عَنْهُ لِأَنَّ دَلَالَةَ الْمَقُولِ بِالْفِعْلِ عَلَى الصَّالِحِ لِأَنْ يُقَالَ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ الْتِزَامٌ وَدَلَالَةُ الِالْتِزَامِ لَيْسَتْ مُعْتَبَرَةً فِي التَّعْرِيفَاتِ لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ لَمْ يُرَدْ بِالْمَقُولِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ إِلَّا الصَّالِحُ لِأَنْ يُقَالَ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ إِذْ لَوْ أُرِيدَ بِهِ الْمَقُولُ بِالْفِعْلِ يَخْرُجُ عَنْ تَعْرِيفِ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ مَفْهُومَاتٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ لَيْسَ لَهَا أَفْرَادٌ مَوْجُودَةٌ فِي الْخَارِجِ وَلَا فِي الذِّهْنِ سَوَاءٌ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهَا أَفْرَادٌ أَصْلًا كَالْكُلِّيَّاتِ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ أَوْ كَانَ لَهَا فَرْدٌ وَاحِدٌ فِي الْخَارِجِ وَالذِّهْنِ بِنَاءً عَلَى بُرْهَانِ امْتِنَاعِ تَعَدُّدِ الْوَاجِبِ خَارِجًا وَذِهْنًا فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَكُونُ مَقُولَةً بِالْفِعْلِ بَلْ بِالصَّلَاحِيَّةِ فَيَكُونُ الْمَقُولُ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ بِمَعْنَى الْكُلِّيِّ. وَأَمَّا مَا أَوْرَدَهُ عَلَيْهِ الدَّوَّانِيُّ أَوَّلًا بِأَنَّ الْكُلِّيَّ هُوَ الَّذِي يُمْكِنُ فَرْضُ الشَّرِكَةِ فِيهِ أَيْ فَرْضُ مَقُولِيَّتِهِ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ وَلَوْ حُمِلَ الْمَقُولُ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ عَلَى مَا يُمْكِنُ فَرْضُ مَقُولِيَّتِهِ لَدَخَلَ فِي التَّعْرِيفِ الْكُلِّيَّاتُ الْفَرْضِيَّةُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْحَقَائِقِ الْمَوْجُودَةِ إِذْ يُمْكِنُ فَرْضُ مَقُولِيَّتِهَا عَلَيْهَا بَلِ الْكُلِّيَّاتُ الْمُتَبَايِنَةُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْمَاهِيَّةِ مُطْلَقًا وَأَمَّا ثَانِيًا فَلِأَنَّ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ الَّتِي لَيْسَتْ لَهَا أَفْرَادٌ أَصْلًا لَيْسَتْ أَجْنَاسًا لِشَيْءٍ فَلَا بَأْسَ بِخُرُوجِهَا وَمِنْ هَهُنَا يَنْقَدِحُ أَنَّ الْمُنْحَصِرَ فِي الْخَمْسِ هُوَ الْكُلِّيَّاتُ الَّتِي لَهَا أَفْرَادٌ
And upon the differentia, because of our need in knowing the proximate and remote differentia for the genus. And upon the species, because the knowledge of a part of the species, which is the relative species, depends on the genus. And he omitted from the definition of genus and the rest of the universals the word 'universal' because 'said of many' suffices for it. Al-Dasuqi said: His statement 'because of our need' etc. means that what is needed must be presented before that which needs it. His statement 'the proximate differentia' like 'rational', which is what distinguishes from the partner in the proximate genus, and 'the remote' which is what distinguishes from the partner in the remote genus, like 'sensitive'. His statement 'because the knowledge of' etc. And that is because he took the genus in the definition of the relative species as will come. Al-'Attar said: And since what is said and predicated is only the universal, not the particular, and such as 'Zayd' is interpreted by this named 'Zayd'. And it was said: Because 'said' means that which is suitable for predication according to actual reality, not according to supposition, and it is more specific than the universal, and from the existence of the more specific follows the existence of the more general. And the author mentioned in the commentary of al-Talkhis that what is said and predicated is only the universal, not the particular. And similarly in the gloss of al-Jalal, for he said: The omission of the word 'universal' is not because the phrase 'said of many' suffices for it, since the universal is its genus, and mentioning the genus is obligatory in complete definitions. The truth is what the commentator said, and it is supported by the statement of al-Sayyid: The concept of 'universal' is the very concept of 'said of many', except that the word 'universal' indicates it summarily, and the phrase 'said of many' indicates it in detail. It cannot be said: The concept of 'universal' is that which is suitable to be said by supposition of many, and the concept of 'said of many' is what is actually said of many, so it does not suffice for it, because the indication of what is actually said upon what is suitable to be said of many is implicational, and implicational indication is not considered in definitions; because we say: By 'said of many' in the definition of the universals nothing but what is suitable to be said of many is meant, since if what is actually said were meant, there would exit from the definition of the universals universal concepts that have no individuals existing in the external or in the mind, whether they have no individuals at all like the hypothetical universals, or they have one individual in the external and mind based on the proof of the impossibility of the multiplicity of the Necessary in external and mind, for they are not actually said, but only by suitability; thus 'said of many' means the universal. As for what al-Dawwani objected against it first: that the universal is that in which the sharing can be supposed, i.e., the supposition of its being said of many; if 'said' in the definition is taken as that whose being said can be supposed, then the hypothetical universals would enter the definition in relation to existing realities, since their being said upon them can be supposed; indeed the mutually exclusive universals in relation to the quiddity absolutely. And second: because the universals that have no individuals at all are not genera for anything, so there is no harm in their exclusion. From this it becomes evident that what is confined to the five are the universals that have individuals.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the priority of genus over other universals and the omission of the term 'universal' (kulli) from their definitions. The author begins by stating that genus is presented before the differentia (fasl) because we need genus to know both the proximate differentia (e.g., 'rational' which distinguishes humans from other animals) and the remote differentia (e.g., 'sensitive' which distinguishes animals from plants). Similarly, genus precedes species (naw') because a part of the species—the relative species (al-naw' al-idafi), such as 'animal' considered as a species relative to 'living body' which is its genus—depends on the genus. The author then notes that the matn omitted the word 'al-kulli' (the universal) from the definitions of genus and the other four universals, arguing that the phrase 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' (said of many) suffices and renders 'kulli' redundant. Al-Dasuqi's commentary expands on why genus must be introduced first: it is a principle needed for understanding other logical concepts. Al-'Attar then discusses a nuance: predication applies only to universals, not particulars; even statements like 'Zayd is rational' involve a universal concept (human) applied to Zayd. A question arises: is 'maqul' (said) equivalent to 'kulli'? Some argue that 'maqul' means actually predicated, while 'kulli' means potentially predicable. Al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani holds that they are identical in concept but differ in expression—'kulli' is summary, 'maqul' is detailed. The passage refutes an objection: if 'maqul' meant actual predication, then universals with no actual individuals (like hypothetical universals) would be excluded from definitions, but these are still usable in logic. Ultimately, the correct view is that 'maqul' in the definitions is understood as 'suitable for predication', making it synonymous with 'kulli'. Al-Dawwani's objection that potential predication admits hypothetical universals is countered: those without individuals are not genera anyway. The lesson thus clarifies the exact relationship between 'kulli' and 'maqul' and justifies the economy of the matn.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' is sufficient to replace 'kulli' in definitions?
A) Because 'maqul' is more specific and implies actual predication
B) Because the concept of 'kulli' is identical to 'maqul' in meaning, just expressed in summary or detail
C) Because 'kulli' is a later innovation
D) Because the matn made a mistake

Key Takeaway
The term 'kulli' is redundant when 'maqul 'ala al-kathra' is understood to mean 'suitable for predication', thus the matn's omission is justified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'mumkin' (possible being): 'that whose existence and non-existence are not impossible'. Identify the universal concept here. Could this definition be expressed without using the word 'kulli'? How?
?
Al-Dawwani objected that 'maqul' as potential predication would include hypothetical universals. How does the author respond? Evaluate the strength of this response.

The Five Universals

10 min
Refining the Definition of Genus: 'Like Jins' Not 'Jins'
Commentator argues that 'jins' should be defined as 'like genus', not as genus itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the commentator replaces 'jins' with 'like jins' in the definition of genus and identify the accidental nature of predicability.

Core Idea
Genus is defined by essence, not by accidental predicability.

In discussing the five universals, the definition of 'genus' (jins) seems straightforward. However, this commentator reveals a subtle but critical flaw: the phrase 'jins' in the definition implies that predicability (maquliyya) is constitutive, when it is actually accidental. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for how we define the first universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The term under scrutiny is 'jins' (genus), which is the first of the five universals. The commentator is critiquing a definition that uses 'jins' itself as part of the definition.
2
Extract the problematic definition
The definition in question is something like 'jins is the universal predicated of many' (al-kulli al-maqul 'ala kathirin). The commentator argues that using 'jins' in the definiens is misleading.
The precise definition being critiqued is not quoted verbatim but is reconstructed from the commentator's objection.
3
Analyze why predicability is accidental
The commentator states that the essence of genus is being an essential universal that enters into the quiddity of its individuals, regardless of whether it is actually predicated. The property 'predicated of many' is an accidental attribute (arid), not part of the essence.
This is a key logical distinction: constitutive vs. accidental properties.
4
Evaluate the commentator's solution
The solution is to replace 'jins' with 'like jins' (ka al-jins). This signals that the definition is describing something analogous to genus, not giving a strict definition, because the defining property is accidental rather than essential.
This ensures that the definition does not imply that predicability constitutes genus.
5
Connect to broader implications
The discussion extends to the nature of predication (haml) and whether universals can be predicated of particulars and vice versa. The commentator presents refutations (naqd ijmail and naqd tafsili) against views that allow symmetric predication, reinforcing the accidental status of predicability.
This shows how a seemingly minor terminological adjustment has far-reaching consequences for logical theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مبادئ التصورات (قوله : (جنس) الأولى أن يقول : كالجنس، وذلك لأن المقولية أمر عارض للمعرف الذي هو الجنس؛ لأنه الكلي الذاتي الداخل في ماهية ما تحته من الحقائق؛ سواء كان يقال عليها أم لا، وأما كونه صالحا لأن يقال عليها، فهو أمر عارض له. العطار على غيره، وقولنا: هذا زيد؛ معناه أن هذا مسمى بزيد، ومدلول لهذا اللفظ أو ذات مشخصة إلى غير ذلك من المفهومات الكلية ا.هـ. ، وأجاز الدواني حمله على جزئي مغاير له بحسب الاعتبار؛ متحد معه بحسب الذات، كما في: هذا الصاحك وهذا الكاتب؛ فإنهما مختلفان بحسب المفهوم، ومتحدان بحسب الذات، فإن ذاتهما زيد بعينه مثلا، وكذا يجوز حمله على كلي آخر في قضية جزئية، كما في قولك: بعض الإنسان زيد.ا.هـ. وقواه أبو الفتح بأن دليل المنع معارض بأن الكلي محمول على الجزئي الحقيقي إيجابا بداهة واتفاقا، كقولنا: زيد إنسان، وهو يدل على كون الجزئي الحقيقي محمولا على الكلي إيجابا؛ ضرورة أن الحمل هو الاتحاد، وهو من الطرفين، ومنقوض نقضا إجماليا؛ بأنه لو تم لدل على بطلانه حمل الكلي على الجزئي الحقيقي، بل على الكلي أيضا؛ لجريان الدليل المذكور فيه، ومنقوض نقضا تفصيليا؛ بأنه أراد بالنفس من جمع لوجوه، نختار أن الجزئي الحقيقي يحمل على غيره بحسب المفهوم والاعتبار، ونمنع امتناعه؛ لجواز اتحاد المفهومين المتغايرين في نظر العقل بحسب الخارج، وإن أراد النفس بوجه ما: نختار أنه يحمل على نفسه، ولا استحالة فيه؛ إذ يكفي في النسبة التغاير الاعتباري؛ خاصة (قوله : كالجنس... إلخ) يؤخذ من تعليله بعده أنه ليس جنساً، ولا كالجنس بل هو وأن الجنس هو الكلي الذاتي المحذوف، المستغنى عنه بالقول، وأيضاً قوله فيما مضى: وهذا التعريف رسم إلى أن قال : لكن المقول على كثيرين أمر عارض له غير مقوم له . ا. هـ. واعلم أن حذف الجنس اكتفاء بخاصته لا يسوغ في التعاريف التامة، ولذا قال المصنف في شرح الأصل: يمنع ما يقال إن ذكر الكلي مستدرك.ا.هـ. ومثله في حاشية الجلال، راجع العطار . ا . هـ. الشرنوبي.
Section on the principles of concepts. (His statement: 'genus') It would be better to say 'like genus', because predicability is an accidental property of the defined entity which is genus; for genus is the essential universal entering into the essence of something under it from among the realities, regardless of whether it is said of them or not. As for its being suitable to be said of them, that is accidental to it. And our statement 'this is Zaid' means that this is named Zaid, and is denoted by this expression or a specific essence, and so on from among the universal concepts. And al-Dawwani permitted predicating it of a particular different from it in terms of consideration but united with it in terms of essence, as in 'this laugher' and 'this writer'; for they differ in concept but are united in essence, for their essence is Zaid himself. Similarly, it is permissible to predicate it of another universal in a particular proposition, as in your statement 'some human is Zaid'. And Abu al-Fath supported this by saying that the evidence for prohibition is counteracted by the fact that the universal is predicated of the real particular affirmatively by intuition and agreement, as in 'Zaid is human', and this indicates that the real particular is predicated of the universal affirmatively, because predication is unification, and it is from both sides. And it is refuted by a general refutation: if it were valid, it would imply the invalidity of predicating the universal of the real particular, and even of the universal as well, because the same evidence applies. And it is refuted by a detailed refutation: if he meant the self by an aspect among aspects, we choose that the real particular is predicated of something else in terms of concept and consideration, and we deny its impossibility, because it is permissible for two different concepts to unite in the view of the mind according to external reality. And if he meant the self by some aspect, we choose that it is predicated of itself, and there is no impossibility, because mere consideration-based diversity suffices for the relation. Especially (His statement: 'like genus' etc.) It is taken from his subsequent explanation that it is not a genus nor like a genus, rather it is that the genus is the essential universal, omitted, and the statement is dispensed with. Also his statement earlier: 'And this definition is a description' until he said: 'But the predicate of many is an accidental property of it, not constitutive of it.' And know that omitting the genus, being satisfied with its specific property, is not permissible in complete definitions. Hence the author said in Sharh al-Asl: 'It prevents what is said that mentioning the universal is redundant.' And similarly in Hashiyat al-Jalal, refer to al-Atar. End. By al-Shurunbali.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the definition of 'genus' (jins) in the context of the five universals. The commentator argues that the phrase 'jins' in the definition should be replaced with 'like jins' (ka al-jins) because the property of being predicated of many (maquliyya) is accidental (arid) to the essence of genus, not constitutive. Genus is defined as the essential universal that enters into the essence of the things under it, regardless of whether it is actually predicated. The ability to be predicated is a secondary attribute. The commentator then discusses the issue of predication (haml) in detail, presenting views from al-Dawwani and Abu al-Fath on whether a universal can be predicated of a particular and vice versa. Al-Dawwani allowed predicating a universal on a particular that differs in consideration (i'tibar) but is identical in essence, such as 'this laugher' and 'this writer' both referring to Zaid. Abu al-Fath supported this by pointing out that if predication is unification from both sides, then 'Zaid is human' shows the particular predicated of the universal as well. The commentator then refutes this via general and detailed refutations (naqd ijmail and naqd tafsili), arguing that the real particular can be predicated of something else in terms of concept, and that self-predication is possible with sufficient consideration-based diversity. The footnote by al-Shurunbali further explains that omitting the genus in definitions is not allowed, and that the mention of 'kulli' is not redundant.

مقولية
حمل
جزئي حقيقي

Excluding Other Universals from Genus Definition
Commentary explaining exclusion of universals from genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two conditions of genus to determine which universals are excluded and explain why each fails.

Core Idea
Two conditions in genus definition exclude all other five universals except genus itself.

In this passage, the commentators explain how the definition of genus (jins) uses two precise conditions to carve out its domain. The first condition, 'different in reality', excludes species, while the second condition, 'in answer to what is it?', excludes the remaining three: differentia, property, and accident general. But as al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar note, there are complications because the first condition also excludes differentia and property if taken literally, and we must factor in the aspect (haythiyya) to avoid confusion. This lesson walks through the logical test for each universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions of genus.
The passage states genus is defined as 'that which is said in answer to what is it of multiple individuals differing in reality'. The two conditions are: (1) said in answer to 'what is it?', and (2) individuals differ in reality.
2
Apply condition 1: 'said in answer to what is it?'
Species also satisfies this condition, so it is not excluded by this condition alone. Differentia and property do not: they answer 'what sort of thing?'. Accident general does not answer 'what is it?' at all (only by expansion).
The passage notes that accident general can be used in answer to 'what is it?' only by necessity, not essentially.
3
Apply condition 2: 'individuals differ in reality'.
Species applies to individuals that agree in reality, not differ, so species is excluded. The passage notes that this condition also seems to exclude differentia and property, but the commentators resolve that the second condition handles them later.
Al-Dasuqi raises this objection; the answer is that the author intended to group proportionally.
4
Understand the objection and resolution.
The objection: the first condition excludes differentia and property as well, so it is arbitrary to single out species. The resolution: the author chose to exclude species with condition 1, and differentia and property with condition 2, which is acceptable because the two conditions together cover all. Al-'Attar adds that the aspect (haythiyya) must be considered to avoid equivocation.
This introduces the concept of haythiyya for deeper analysis.

قوله : يخرج الكليات الباقية أي : لأن قوله : في جواب يخرج العرض العام؛ لأنه لا يقال في الجواب، وقوله : ما هو؟ يخرج الفصل والخاصة؛ لأنهما يقالان في جواب أي شيء.
(العطار) قوله : يخرج النوع) قيل : تخصيص الإخراج به تحكم، فإنه كما يخرجه يخرج خاصته وفصله القريب، وأُجيب بأنه قصد جمع المتناسبات في الإخراج بقيد واحد، ثم إن الشارح لم يتكلم على قيد الحيثية؛ أي: من حيث هو كذلك لعدم التصريح به، وإلا؛ فلا بد من اعتباره في تعريف الكليات؛ لأنها أمور إضافية تختلف بالاعتبار، وتتصادق على شيء واحد، ومثلوا لذلك بالملون؛ أي: ذي اللون؛ فإنه جنس للأسود لصدقه عليه وعلى الأصفر والأخضر ونحوهما، وهذه الأفراد مختلفة بالحقيقة، ونوع من الكيف، فإنه يشمل الكيف بالنعومة والحلاوة مثلاً من بقية أنواع الكيفيات المحسوسة، وفصل للكثيف؛ أي: الجسم الكثيف، فإن الجسم جنس البسيط الذي لا لون له، وللكثيف الملوَّن، وخاصة للجسم؛ فإن الجوهر الفرد لا لون له، وعرض عام للحيوان؛ لعدم اختصاصه بنوع دون نوع. وبقية الكلام في حواشينا على الولددية.
قوله : يخرج الكليات الباقية أما العرض العام؛ فيخرج بقوله: (في جواب)؛ لأنه لا يقال في الجواب أصلاً، ووقوعه في جواب: كيف زيد؛ بأن يقال: صحيح مثلاً، ليس معتبراً عندهم، فهو يقع في جواب ما هو على سبيل التوسع والاضطرار، قال الدواني في حاشية الشرح الجديد على التجريد: الرسم يقع في مطلب ما هو على سبيل التوسع والاضطرار؛ كما صرح به في شرح الإشارات، ولا منافاة بينه وبين ما اشتهر في كلامهم من حضر المقول في جواب ما هو في الأمور الثلاثة، فإن هذا الحصر إنما هو بحسب الحقيقة اهـ. والباقي تخرج بقوله: ما هو؛ لأن ما هو سؤال عن الحقيقة، فلا إيجاب بما ليس ماهية.
Chapter: On the principles of concepts. And by his statement: 'different in reality' the species is excluded. And by his statement: 'in answer to what is it?' the remaining universals are excluded. (Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'the species is excluded' contains that the proximate differentia like 'rational' and the property of the species like 'laughing' are also excluded. The answer: they are indeed excluded by that condition, but the author later excludes them with his statement: 'in answer to what is it?' so the commentator followed him on that. His statement: 'the remaining universals are excluded' means: his statement 'in answer' excludes the accident general because it is not said in answer; and his statement 'what is it?' excludes the differentia and property because they are said in answer to 'what sort of thing'. (Al-'Attar: His statement 'the species is excluded') It was said: specifying its exclusion is arbitrary, for just as it excludes it, it excludes its property and its proximate differentia. The answer is that he intended to group proportional things in exclusion with one condition. Moreover, the commentator did not talk about the condition of 'from the perspective' i.e. from the perspective that it is such, due to lack of explicit mention; otherwise, it must be considered in the definition of the universals because they are relational matters that differ by consideration and apply to one thing. Examples given: 'colored' i.e. possessing color; it is a genus for black because it applies to it and to yellow, green etc., and these individuals differ in reality; and a species of quality because it includes the quality of softness and sweetness from the remaining types of sensible qualities; and a differentia for dense i.e. dense body, because body is the genus of simple which has no color, and of dense colored; and a property for body because the individual substance has no color; and an accident general for animal because it is not restricted to one species. The rest of the discussion is in our glosses on al-Waladiyya. His statement: 'the remaining universals are excluded' As for the accident general, it is excluded by his statement: 'in answer' because it is not said in answer at all; and its occurrence in answer to 'how is Zaid?' by saying 'healthy' is not considered by them; it occurs in answer to 'what is it?' by way of expansion and necessity. Al-Dawani said in his gloss on the new commentary on al-Tajrid: the description occurs in the demand for 'what is it?' by way of expansion and necessity, as stated in Sharh al-Isharat; and there is no contradiction between that and what is known in their speech about the presence of the predicated in answer to 'what is it?' in the three matters, for this restriction is only according to reality. And the rest are excluded by his statement: 'what is it?' because 'what is it?' is a question about reality, so nothing that is not essence is affirmed.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of genus, explaining how its two conditions exclude the remaining four universals. The author mentions a chapter on the principles of concepts, then states that by the condition 'different in reality' the species is excluded, and by the condition 'in answer to what is it?' the other universals (differentia, property, accident general) are excluded. Al-Dasuqi raises an objection: the first condition also excludes the proximate differentia and property, not just species. The answer is that the author intended to exclude them later with the second condition. The commentator then explains for each universal: the accident general is excluded because it is never said in answer to 'what is it?' (only in answer to 'how?'), while the differentia and property are excluded because they are said in answer to 'what sort of thing?' not 'what is it?'. Al-'Attar expands on the objection and answer, emphasizing that the definitions of universals must consider the aspect (haythiyya) because one thing can be multiple universals under different aspects. He gives the example of 'colored' (mulawwan): it is a genus for black, a species of quality, a differentia for dense body, a property for body, and an accident general for animal. This illustrates how the same term can exclude or include different universals depending on the perspective. The passage then concludes that the accident general is only used in answer to 'what is it?' by way of necessity, not essentially, so it remains excluded.

One Term, Five Roles: The Principle of Haythiyya
Expansion on the concept of haythiyya through mulawwan example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to demonstrate how the same term can represent different universals under different aspects (haythiyya) by analyzing a multi-role example.

Core Idea
A term's universal status depends on perspective, not just its inherent meaning.

Al-'Attar introduces an important refinement: the definitions of the five universals must consider the aspect (haythiyya) because one and the same thing can be a genus, species, differentia, property, or accident general under different perspectives. The classic example is 'mulawwan' (colored), which takes on all five roles depending on what it is being compared to. This lesson unpacks that example to train your mind in relational thinking.

Guided Path
1
Read the example of 'mulawwan' from the passage.
The passage states: 'colored is a genus for black, a species of quality, a differentia for dense body, a property for body, and an accident general for animal.' Note that each role depends on what 'mulawwan' is being compared to.
2
For each universal role, identify the relatum.
As genus: relatum is black and other colors (individuals differing in reality). As species: relatum is quality (broader genus). As differentia: relatum is dense body (distinguishes from simple body). As property: relatum is body (specific to body, since individual substance lacks color). As accident: relatum is animal (not exclusive to one species).
The same term 'colored' is each universal because of the different aspects (haythiyyat).
3
Construct a similar analysis for an Islamic term.
Choose 'natiq' (rational). In Kalam: Natiq is a differentia for 'insan' (human) because it distinguishes human from other animals. It is a property for human (only humans are rational). It is an accident general for 'jism' (body) because not all bodies are rational. It might be a species of 'kalam' (speech) if considered as a type of speech? Actually, careful: choose a term that fits better. Use 'jism' (body) as in the passage's extension.
Ensure the example is from Islamic science; 'insan' and 'natiq' are used in Kalam.
4
Apply the condition checks for each role.
For each role of 'jism', test the two conditions of genus: is it said in answer to 'what is it?' and do its individuals differ in reality? This will confirm whether it fits that role.
This step connects back to the previous lesson's conditions.

Distinguishing Near and Distant Genus
Author defines the conditions for a genus to be near or distant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given genus is near or distant by applying the criterion of uniform answer across all participants.

Core Idea
A genus is near if it answers equally for the essence and all its sharers.

In defining any essence, we place it under a genus. But not all genera are equally precise: some are near (qarīb) and some are distant (baʿīd). This passage gives the exact criterion for distinguishing them, using 'animal' as a model example. Understanding this distinction is crucial for crafting complete definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the criterion
The author states: if the answer to 'what is it?' for the essence and for some participants is the same answer for all participants, then the genus is near. Focus on the uniformity requirement across all members of that genus.
2
Apply to the animal example
Test 'animal' as a genus for 'human'. Does 'animal' answer 'what is human?' Yes. Does it also answer 'what is horse?' Yes. And for every other species that shares the genus 'animal' (donkey, camel, etc.), the answer is still 'animal'. Therefore, 'animal' is a near genus.
The passage explicitly asks: 'What are human and horse?' and 'What are human, horse, donkey, camel?' The answer is always 'animal'.
3
Contrast with distant genus
Now consider 'growing body'. It also answers 'what is human?' but does it answer uniformly for all participants in 'animal'? No, because the set of growing bodies includes plants, not just animals. So it is a distant genus.
The criterion fails because the answer is not the same for all members of the intended genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ كَانَ الجَوَابُ عَن المَاهِيَّةِ وَعَنْ بَعْضِ المُشَارَكَاتِ، هُوَ الجَوَابَ عَنْهَا وَعَنِ الكُلِّ؛ فَقَرِيْبٌ كَالْحَيَوَانِ. فَإِنَّه جواب عن الإنسان وعن بعض مشاركاته في الحيوانية، كالفرس مثلاً، وكذلك جواب عنه وعن جميع مشاركاته في الحيوانية. فإذا قيلَ: ما الإنسانُ والفرسُ؟ كان الجواب: الحيوان. وإذا قيل: ما الإنسانُ والفرسُ والحمارُ والجَملُ إلى غير ذلك؟ كان الجواب: الحيوان. وَإِلَّا فَبَعِيدٌ كَالْجِسْمِ النَّامِي.
If the answer about the essence and about some of the sharers is the same answer about it and about all, then it is near, like 'animal'. For it is an answer about 'human' and about some of its sharers in animality, like 'horse' for example, and likewise an answer about it and about all its sharers in animality. So if it is said: 'What is human and horse?' the answer is 'animal'. And if it is said: 'What is human and horse and donkey and camel and so on?' the answer is 'animal'. Otherwise, it is distant, like 'growing body'.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between a near genus (jins qarīb) and a distant genus (jins baʿīd). The author begins by stating a condition: if a single answer (jawāb) to the question 'what is it?' (mā huwa) applies both to the essence (māhiyyah) being defined and to some of its participants (mushārakāt) in that genus, and that same answer also applies to all participants (al-kull), then that answer is a near genus. The example given is 'animal' (al-ḥayawān): it is the answer for 'human' (al-insān) and for some sharers like 'horse' (al-faras), and also for all animals such as donkey, camel, etc. Two scenarios illustrate this: asking 'What are human and horse?' gets 'animal'; asking 'What are human, horse, donkey, camel, etc.?' also gets 'animal'. If the condition is not met—i.e., the answer for the essence and some sharers is not the same as for all sharers—then the genus is distant, like 'growing body' (al-jism al-nāmī) which is a broader category. The passage clarifies that nearness is determined by the scope of the answer covering all individuals under that genus, whereas a distant genus only covers a subset.

الجنس البعيد

A near genus provides a single, uniform answer for the defined essence and all other species that share that genus.

Proximal vs Distant Genus: The Complete Common Notion
Defines proximal and distant genus and critiques a misinterpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish proximal genus from distant genus using the scope of the answer and the concept of tamām al-mushtarak.

Core Idea
Proximal genus covers all co-participants; distant genus covers only some.

In this passage, al-‘Aṭṭār refines the definition of proximal and distant genus. He clarifies that the key is not merely being the immediate higher genus, but whether the answer about the quiddity and its co-participants is the same for all. This corrects a potential misinterpretation by the commentator (al-Dasūqī) and provides a precise tool for definition analysis in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quiddity and its co-participants
In the example, the quiddity is human, and co-participants include horse, donkey, etc., in the attribute of animality. Note which attribute is being shared.
2
Test the answer's scope
When asked 'What is human and horse?', the answer 'animal' works. When asked about human and donkey, the answer is still 'animal'. This shows the answer is the same for all co-participants in animality.
3
Apply the complete common notion test
The proximal genus is the complete common notion (tamām al-mushtarak) between the quiddity and all its co-participants. Check if the proposed genus covers all shared attributes fully. For human and horse, 'animal' covers sensation, growth, voluntary movement—everything they share as animals.
4
Differentiate from distant genus
If the answer differs for different groups of co-participants, the genus is distant. For example, if we compare human and plant, the answer 'animal' is not common. The distant genus is the complete common notion between the quiddity and only some co-participants.
5
Apply the rule from al-Sayyid
Count all answers that cover all participants, subtract one, and the remainder is the rank of the distant answer. This quantifies how many steps away a genus is.

Some of its co-participants in animality, like the horse for example, and likewise an answer about it and about all its co-participants in animality. So if it is said: 'What is human and horse?' The answer is 'animal'. And if it is said: 'What is human, horse, donkey, camel, etc.?' The answer is 'animal'. (Otherwise) i.e., if the answer about the quiddity and about some of what [...] al-Dasūqī: The apparent meaning of the commentator is that it is the collective (majmū‘ī) because he answered with it about the whole, when he said: 'What is human and horse... etc.' at one time, so it implies that growing body is also proximal because it occurs as an answer. Reflect on it, for there is something in it. (His statement: 'And if the answer about the quiddity is not... etc.') i.e., rather the answer differs, so the answer about it and about some co-participants is different from the answer about it and about other co-participants. al-Quṭb said: So there are two answers: if the genus is distant by one rank, like growing body relative to man, then 'animal' is one answer, and it is another answer; or three answers if distant by two ranks, like body in relation to it, then 'animal' and 'growing body' are two answers, and it is a third answer; or four answers if distant by three ranks, and so on. al-Sayyid said: The rule for knowing the distant [genus] is to consider the number of answers comprehensive of all co-participants, subtract one, and what remains is the rank of the answer. al-‘Aṭṭār: (His statement: 'The apparent meaning of the commentator...') is refuted by the commentator's statement: 'all its co-participants in animality', and his statement 'etc.', for it is explicit in intending the comprehensive whole (kull jumū‘ī), not the collective (majmū‘ī), as is not hidden from one who reflects. What compelled him to this is the confusion of the glosses in this topic. What clarifies it is his exposition that by 'proximal genus' is meant the complete common notion between the quiddity and all that shares with it, unlike the distant [genus] which is the complete common notion between them and some of what shares with it, not all of it. Thus the inclusion of growing body in the definition of proximal does not arise. End. al-Shurunbulālī.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage defines proximal and distant genus and includes a critical refinement of the commentator's (shāriḥ) interpretation. The central idea: if the answer to a question about a quiddity (māhiyya) and its co-participants in a given attribute is the same for all, then that answer is a proximal genus (jins qarīb). For example, 'animal' answers both 'What is human?' and 'What is horse?' because they share in animality (ḥayawāniyya). If the answer differs when considering different co-participants, then the genus is distant (jins ba‘īd). The commentator initially seemed to treat the genus as collective (majmū‘ī), implying that 'growing body' (jism nāmī) could be proximal for human and horse since it also applies to them. However, al-‘Aṭṭār refutes this, clarifying that the proximal genus is the complete common notion (tamām al-mushtarak) between the quiddity and all its co-participants. 'Growing body' is not proximal because it does not cover the full animality (sensation and voluntary movement) that horse shares with human. The rule from al-Sayyid: for a distant genus, count all answers covering all participants, subtract one, and the remainder is the rank of the distant answer. This lesson guards against conflating 'applying to all participants' with 'being the complete common notion covering all participants'.

Distant vs Proximal Genus: The Growing Body Example
Illustrates why growing body is a distant genus for human and horse but proximal for human and tree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a distant and proximal genus by identifying the complete common notion between two items.

Core Idea
A genus is proximal only when it captures the most specific shared attribute.

This passage from the Tadhheeb al-Mantiq explains a critical nuance in defining genus. Using the example of 'growing body,' it shows how a single term can be a proximal genus for some comparisons but distant for others. Understanding this distinction is essential for constructing precise definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two comparisons
The passage compares human with tree, and human with horse. Note the attribute each pair shares: human and tree share 'growing body'; human and horse share 'growing body' but also 'animality' (sensation and voluntary movement).
2
Determine the complete common notion (tamām al-mushtarak)
For human and tree, the only shared attribute relevant to the question is 'growing body', so that is the complete common notion. For human and horse, they share more: animality, so animal (growing, sentient, moving) is the complete common notion.
The complete common notion is the most specific attribute that applies to all individuals.
3
Apply the rule: answer with the most specific common attribute
The correct answer to 'What is X and Y?' must use the most specific attribute that applies to both. That attribute is the proximal genus for that pair.
Al-Dasūqī emphasizes: 'the answer about multiple things is only with the complete common notion.'
4
Recognize distant vs. proximal
If a shared attribute is not the most specific, it is a distant genus. 'Growing body' is a distant genus for human and horse because they share animality, which is more specific.
Al-‘Aṭṭār adds that the same attribute can be proximal for one pair and distant for another.

(قَوْلُهُ : الَّتِي هِيَ عِبَارَةٌ) أي : معبّر عنها بالجسم النامي ... إلخ؛ لأنَّ الحيوانية معنى يعبر عنها بما ذكر، وليس المرادُ أنَّها لفظ يعبر به عمَّا ذكر . العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : كَالْجِسْمِ النَّامِي) يقعُ في الجوابِ عَنِ النَّباتِ والإنسانِ إذا سُئِلَ عنهُمَا بِمَا هوَ، وهو بعينِهِ جواب لِلسُّؤال عن النَّباتِ، وعن كُلِّ واحدٍ واحدٍ مِمَّا يُشاركُهُ فيه، فهو جنس قريب لِلنَّباتِ، وبعيدٌ لِلإنسان إذا سُئِلَ عنه وعن النَّبَاتِ بِمَا هو . For it occurs as an answer about man and about what shares with him in growing body only, not about what shares with him in animality. So if it is said: 'What is man and tree?', the growing body occurs in the answer. But if it is said: 'What is man and horse?', it does not occur—despite both sharing in growing body—because the horse does not share with man in growing body only, but shares with him in animality, which is defined as the growing body that is sentient. al-Dasūqī: (His statement: 'like the growing body') its summary: that it occurs as an answer to the question about the human quiddity and about some of what shares with it, namely the tree. So if it is said: 'What is man and tree?', it is said: 'A growing body', and it does not occur as an answer about the quiddity and about all that shares with it. Do you not see that the horse and donkey share with man in growing body, yet it does not occur as an answer to the question about them? Because the answer about multiple things is only with the complete common notion, and the complete common notion between man, donkey, and horse is only animal, or a growing body that is sentient and moves by will.
