The Concept of Isolated Genus
Author and commentators discuss the isolated genus as an exception to the ascending chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define an isolated genus, identify its criteria, and apply them to the example of the absolute intellect.

Core Idea
An isolated genus has no genus above it and no genus below it.

After learning that genera are not always arranged in a chain, we now focus on the exception: the isolated genus. Both al-Dasuqi and al-Attar describe it clearly: a genus that has no higher genus above it and no lower genus beneath it—only species. The example of the absolute intellect from Islamic Neoplatonism showcases how this concept is applied in real philosophical discourse.

Guided Path
1
Find the description of isolated genus in al-Dasuqi's commentary.
Identify the phrase 'al-jins al-mufrad' in the Arabic. Note that al-Dasuqi defines it as 'that which has no genus above it and no genus below it, but below it are species.'
This is a rasm (descriptive definition), not a hadd haqiqi.
2
Extract the two criteria: no higher genus and no lower genus.
Write down: (1) There is no genus above it (i.e., it is not a species of a higher genus). (2) There is no genus below it (i.e., it does not have sub-genera). It only has species directly under it.
Al-Attar adds the analogy with relative species, reinforcing the criteria.
3
Analyze the example of the absolute intellect.
Al-Dasuqi gives the 'aql muṭlaq as an example. According to one view, substance is not its genus, so it has no higher genus. The ten intellects under it are species, not sub-genera, because they differ by differentiae. So it satisfies both criteria.
This shows that even a respected concept in Islamic philosophy can be an isolated genus.
4
Contrast with a typical genus in a chain.
Compare with a genus like 'animal' which has a higher genus ('living body') and lower genera ('rational', 'non-rational' if considered sub-genera). This helps solidify the definition of isolated genus.
Understanding this distinction prevents misclassification.

The Genus of Genera: Definition and Example
Author defines jins al-ajnas with animal example and discusses substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the jins al-ajnas in a given hierarchy and explain why it is called so.

Core Idea
The genus of genera is the highest category with no genus above it.

The concept of 'jins al-ajnas' is foundational to understanding the Porphyrian tree. In this passage, the author defines it and gives a classic example. Al-Dasuqi's commentary adds depth by discussing whether substance qualifies as such. Let's dissect this definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find 'جنس الأجناس' in the Arabic. What is it called? It is the highest genus.
2
Find the example
The author gives 'الحيوان' (animal) as an example of jins al-ajnas. But is animal truly the highest? Note that in the Greek tradition, substance is the highest. The author acknowledges this by referring to substance.
3
Examine the condition for jinsiyya
Al-Dasuqi states that genericity is based on universality, and being said in answer to 'what is it?' by the intellect. This links to the quiddity.
4
Understand the debate about substance
There are two views: some say substance is not a genus for the intellects, others say it is. This affects how we understand the hierarchy.

(And it is called) that highest (genus of genera) like animal for example, for it is a genus. Al-Dasuqi (his saying: It is called genus of genera. The highest of genera is called genus of genera because the genericity of a thing is by virtue of universality after it is said in answer to al-Attar by the intellect; based on the fact that substance is not a genus for it, and that the ten intellects are different in essence, or based on the fact that substance is a genus for it, and that the ten intellects are united in essence. One of. (1) (His saying: like animal... etc.) We clarify for you the matter with comprehensive examples other than what tongues have chewed and ears have become bored of, by limiting the example to animal and substance for the lowest and highest genera, and to body and man for the highest and lowest species, and to what is between the first two, namely growing body and body, for the intermediate genera, and to what is between the last two, namely growing body and animal, for the intermediate species, so that the observer thinks that they have not found another example. So we say, and with God is success: Plant is a genus under which there are many types that man cannot count, such as wheat, corn, rice, dates, cotton, vegetables, flowers... etc., and each of these types has many subtypes under it. So absolute wheat has under it Indian, local, Australian, etc.; corn has under it local, 'uwayja, nab al-jamal, etc.; rice has under it Yabani, Sultani, 'ayn al-bint, etc.; dates have under them zaghlul, samani, hayani, etc.; cotton has under it siklaredi, Giza, ashmuni; vegetables have under them carrots, turnips, radishes, etc.; flowers have under them roses, narcissus, jasmine, etc. So Indian wheat is like man: a real and relative species. As for its being real, because under it are individuals, and it is the complete quiddity; as for its being relative, because it falls under absolute wheat along with its other types. And what is said about wheat applies also to corn, rice... etc. Hence we see firstly that Indian wheat, 'uwayja corn, Giza cotton, etc. are lowest species like man, because they are the most specific species and under them are individuals. Secondly, absolute wheat, absolute corn, absolute cotton, etc. are proximate lowest genera and intermediate species. As for their being proximate lowest, because they are the most specific genera like animal; as for their being intermediate species, because their real species fall under them, and they fall under a third higher type, namely absolute plant. And this type is an intermediate type and genus because others fall under it and it falls under a fourth higher type, namely growing body. And this type is an intermediate type and genus because others fall under it and it falls under a fifth higher type, namely absolute body. And this type is the highest type and an intermediate genus. As for its being the highest, because there is no type above it; as for its being an intermediate genus, because...
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of 'jins al-ajnas' (genus of genera). The main text states that the highest genus is called 'jins al-ajnas', and gives 'animal' as an example. Al-Dasuqi explains that the highest genus is so called because its genericity is based on the broadest universality, and he discusses a subtlety regarding whether 'jawhar' (substance) is a genus for the intellects. Then the commentator provides a detailed concrete example using plants to illustrate the hierarchical structure of genera and species. He points out that the standard examples (animal, man, body, substance) have become overused, so he offers a fresh example from botany. He lists types of plants such as wheat, corn, rice, etc., and shows how each can be subdivided into more specific types, forming a hierarchy. He distinguishes between 'naw' haqiqi' (real species) which has only individuals under it, and 'naw' idafi' (relative species) which is both a species relative to higher genera and a genus relative to lower species. The example shows five levels: the lowest species (e.g., Indian wheat), absolute wheat as a proximate genus and intermediate species, then absolute plant, then growing body, then absolute body as the highest type but still an intermediate genus because above it is substance, which is the true jins al-ajnas. This illustrates the principle of ascending genera and descending species.

Key Takeaway
Jins al-ajnas is the summum genus, the highest category without a superior genus.

The Plant Hierarchy: Illustrating Genera and Species
Author provides a detailed plant example to illustrate genus-species hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real species and a relative species in a given hierarchy, and construct a similar hierarchy for a different domain.

Core Idea
A single level can be both a species (relative to above) and a genus (relative to below).

The plant example is a rich illustration of how a hierarchy of genera and species works. It shows that terms like 'absolute wheat' are ambiguous: they are species relative to 'plant' but genera relative to 'Indian wheat'. This dual nature is crucial for understanding classification in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the lowest species
Find terms like 'Indian wheat' or 'Giza cotton'. These are real species (naw' haqiqi) because they have only individual plants under them, no further types.
2
Identify the proximate genera
Look at 'absolute wheat' or 'absolute cotton'. These are called 'proximate lowest genera' because they are the most specific genera (like animal in the animal hierarchy). They are also intermediate species because they are under a higher genus (absolute plant).
3
Trace the hierarchy upward
Follow the chain: absolute wheat → absolute plant → growing body → absolute body → substance. Each step is a genus relative to the ones below and a species relative to the one above.
4
Understand 'naw' haqiqi' vs 'naw' idafi'
Indian wheat is a real species because it is the complete quiddity (tamam mahiyya) of that type. In contrast, absolute wheat is a relative species because it is defined in relation to both its superiors and inferiors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وَيُسَمَّى) ذلك العالي (جِنْسَ الأَجْنَاسِ) كالحيوان(۱) مثلاً، فإنَّه جنس الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: وَيُسمَّى جِنْسَ الْأَجْنَاسِ إِنَّما كان العالي من الأجناس يُسمى بجنس الأجناس؛ لأنَّ جنسيَّةَ الشَّيء باعتبارِ العموم بعد أن يكون مقولاً في جواب العطار ما بالعقل؛ بناءً على أنَّ الجوهر ليسَ جِنْساً لَهُ وأنَّ العقول العشرةَ مُختلفة الحقيقة، أو بناءً على أنَّ الجوهر جنس له، وأنَّ العقول العشرةَ مُتَّفقةُ بالحقيقة . واحد من ، (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : كالحيوان ... إلخ) نوضح لك المقام بأمثلة جامعة غير ما لاكثه الألسنة ومجته الأسماع من تخصيص التمثيل بحيوان وجوهر لأسفل الأجناس وأعلاها، وبجسم وإنسان لأعلى الأنواع وأسفلها، وبما بين الأوَّلين من الجسم النَّامي والجسم للمتوسط من الأجناس، وبما بين الآخرين من الجسم النامي والحيوان للمتوسط من الأنواع حتى يظن الناظر إليه أنهم لم يعثروا على مثال آخر. فنقول وبالله التوفيق: النبات جنس تحته أنواع كثيرة لا يحصيها الإنسان من قمح، وذرة، وأرز وبلح وقطن وبقل وزهر ... إلخ، وكل هذه الأنواع تحته أنواع كثيرة، فمطلق القمح تحته الهندي والبلدي والإسترالي ونحوها، والذرة تحته البلدي والعويجة وناب الجمل ونحوها، والأرز تحته اليباني والسلطاني وعين البنت ونحوها والبلح تحته الزغلول والسماني والحياني ونحوها، والقطن تحته السكلاريدي والجيزة والأشموني والبقل تحته الجزر واللفت والفجل ونحوها، والزهر تحته الورد والنرجس والفل ونحوها فالقمح الهندي كالإنسان نوع حقيقي وإضافي، أما كونه حقيقياً؛ فلأنَّ ما تحته أشخاص، وهو تمام ماهيته، وأما كونه إضافياً، فلاندراجه مع بقية وثانياً أن مطلق قمح ومطلق ذرة ومطلق قطن ... إلخ أجناس قريبة سافلة وأنواع متوسطة، أما كونها قريبة سافلة؛ فلأنَّها أخص الأجناس كالحيوان، وأما كونها أنواعاً متوسطة؛ فلاندراج أنواعها الحقيقية تحتها، ولاندراجها تحت نوع ثالث أعلى منها، وهو مطلق نبات وهذا النوع نوع و جنس متوسطان لاندراج غيره تحته ولاندراجه هو تحت نوع رابع أعلى منه وهو جسم نامي وهذا النوع نوع وجنس متوسطان لاندراج غيره تحته ولاندراجه (And it is called) that highest (genus of genera) like animal for example, for it is a genus. Al-Dasuqi (his saying: It is called genus of genera. The highest of genera is called genus of genera because the genericity of a thing is by virtue of universality after it is said in answer to al-Attar by the intellect; based on the fact that substance is not a genus for it, and that the ten intellects are different in essence, or based on the fact that substance is a genus for it, and that the ten intellects are united in essence. One of. (1) (His saying: like animal... etc.) We clarify for you the matter with comprehensive examples other than what tongues have chewed and ears have become bored of, by limiting the example to animal and substance for the lowest and highest genera, and to body and man for the highest and lowest species, and to what is between the first two, namely growing body and body, for the intermediate genera, and to what is between the last two, namely growing body and animal, for the intermediate species, so that the observer thinks that they have not found another example. So we say, and with God is success: Plant is a genus under which there are many types that man cannot count, such as wheat, corn, rice, dates, cotton, vegetables, flowers... etc., and each of these types has many subtypes under it. So absolute wheat has under it Indian, local, Australian, etc.; corn has under it local, 'uwayja, nab al-jamal, etc.; rice has under it Yabani, Sultani, 'ayn al-bint, etc.; dates have under them zaghlul, samani, hayani, etc.; cotton has under it siklaredi, Giza, ashmuni; vegetables have under them carrots, turnips, radishes, etc.; flowers have under them roses, narcissus, jasmine, etc. So Indian wheat is like man: a real and relative species. As for its being real, because under it are individuals, and it is the complete quiddity; as for its being relative, because it falls under absolute wheat along with its other types. And what is said about wheat applies also to corn, rice... etc. Hence we see firstly that Indian wheat, 'uwayja corn, Giza cotton, etc. are lowest species like man, because they are the most specific species and under them are individuals. Secondly, absolute wheat, absolute corn, absolute cotton, etc. are proximate lowest genera and intermediate species. As for their being proximate lowest, because they are the most specific genera like animal; as for their being intermediate species, because their real species fall under them, and they fall under a third higher type, namely absolute plant. And this type is an intermediate type and genus because others fall under it and it falls under a fourth higher type, namely growing body. And this type is an intermediate type and genus because others fall under it and it falls under a fifth higher type, namely absolute body. And this type is the highest type and an intermediate genus. As for its being the highest, because there is no type above it; as for its being an intermediate genus, because...
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of 'jins al-ajnas' (genus of genera). The main text states that the highest genus is called 'jins al-ajnas', and gives 'animal' as an example. Al-Dasuqi explains that the highest genus is so called because its genericity is based on the broadest universality, and he discusses a subtlety regarding whether 'jawhar' (substance) is a genus for the intellects. Then the commentator provides a detailed concrete example using plants to illustrate the hierarchical structure of genera and species. He points out that the standard examples (animal, man, body, substance) have become overused, so he offers a fresh example from botany. He lists types of plants such as wheat, corn, rice, etc., and shows how each can be subdivided into more specific types, forming a hierarchy. He distinguishes between 'naw' haqiqi' (real species) which has only individuals under it, and 'naw' idafi' (relative species) which is both a species relative to higher genera and a genus relative to lower species. The example shows five levels: the lowest species (e.g., Indian wheat), absolute wheat as a proximate genus and intermediate species, then absolute plant, then growing body, then absolute body as the highest type but still an intermediate genus because above it is substance, which is the true jins al-ajnas. This illustrates the principle of ascending genera and descending species.

Key Terms
نوع حقيقي
نوع إضافي
جنس قريب
جنس بعيد

C) The summum genus.
D) An individual plant.

Key Takeaway
A single concept can be both a genus and a species depending on its position in the hierarchy.

The Five Universals

Relative vs Real Species: Why Only Relative Species Form a Hierarchy
Author explains the hierarchical arrangement of relative species and the impossibility of arranging real species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between relative and real species and explain why real species cannot be arranged in a hierarchy.

Core Idea
Relative species are genera in disguise; real species cannot be both a species and a genus.

In this passage, the author explains that genera ascend in a hierarchy, and similarly relative species can be arranged. But real species—those with only individuals under them—cannot have another real species above them, because that would turn them into genera. This is a critical distinction in the five universals. We will unpack why this is so and look at examples like 'absolute intellect' as a singular species. The commentary from al-Dasuqi, al-Attar, and al-Sharnubi adds depth to the argument and provides a full hierarchical summary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of species
From the passage, find the terms 'نوع إضافي' and 'نوع حقيقي'. Note that the author uses 'إضافية' to restrict relative species. Write down their definitions: relative species can be a species to a higher genus and a genus to lower species; real species have only individuals under them.
The term 'نوع إضافي' is broader and includes singular species that may not be arranged.
2
Understand why real species cannot be arranged
The author argues that if real species were arranged, the lower one would become a genus, leading to individuals being both identical and different in essence. Read the passage: 'لو ترتبت لكان النوع الحقيقي جنساً' and 'يلزم عليه من كون الأفراد متَّفقة الحقيقة مختلفتها وهو تناقض'.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Examine the singular species exception
The text mentions 'النوع المفرد' like 'العقل المطلق' which has no genus above it and only individuals under it. This is a relative species that is not arranged because it has no higher genus.
This shows that not all relative species are part of an ascending hierarchy.
4
Connect to the five-level hierarchy summary
Al-Sharnubi summarizes that genera have five levels ascending and species have five levels descending. Identify the highest (جوهر) and lowest (القمح الهندي) and the intermediates.
This illustrates the hierarchical tree of Porphyry used in Islamic logic.

(قَوْلُهُ: فَالْجَوْهَرُ جِنْسُ الْأَجْنَاسِ لا يُقالُ: كيف يكون كذلك مع كونه تحت شيء، ومذكور وموجود؟ ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : مَا ذكر لا يصلح أن يكون جنساً عالياً للجوهر؛ لفهمه دونَه، ولو كانَ جنساً له ؛ لتوقف فهمه على فَهُم مَا ذكرَ ؛ ضرورةً توقُفِ فَهُم المركّب على فَهُم أجزائه، وحينَئِذٍ فَمَا ذكرَ عرضُ عام للجوهر . Chapter: On the principles of conceptions. Above it is a genus, which is the growing body, and above it body, and above it substance, so substance is the genus of genera. And just as genera may be arranged ascending, so too are the relative species. Al-Dasuqi: So what is most general of all is called the genus of genera, and what is most specific of all, which is what is under them, is called the lowest genus. (His saying: So substance is the genus of genera. It is not said: How can it be so when it is under something, namely mentioned and existent? Because we say: What is mentioned is not fit to be a higher genus for substance, because it is understood by itself without it, and if it were a genus for it, then understanding it would depend on understanding what is mentioned, necessarily because understanding the compound depends on understanding its parts. Therefore, what is mentioned is a common accident for substance.) (His saying: Likewise, relative species may be arranged. He restricts by 'relative' to exclude real species, for it is impossible for them to be arranged such that a real species is under another real species, because if they were arranged, the real species would be a genus, which is impossible due to the necessary consequence that the individuals to which they are applied would be both identical in essence and different, which is a contradiction. And he brought 'qad' in his saying 'may be arranged' because some relative species have no arrangement, like the singular species, which is that which has no genus above it and under it individuals identical in essence, such as absolute intellect, based on the fact that... Al-Attar: (His saying: the relative) As for the real, it is impossible for them to be arranged, otherwise the real species would be a genus, which is false. Therefore, what is under it falls under it and it falls under substance, which is the genus of genera. And by the examples we mentioned, you learn that genera have five levels arranged ascending, and species have five levels arranged descending, and that the five former are three sections: the first is the most specific, which is absolute wheat, corn, etc.; the second is the highest, which is substance; the third is intermediate, which are three: plant, growing body, and body. And the latter five are also three sections: the first is the highest species, which is body; the second is the most specific, which is Indian wheat, 'uwayja corn, etc.; the third is intermediate between them, also three: absolute wheat or corn, etc., and plant and growing body. End of al-Sharnubi's commentary. And it is...
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage from a chapter on the principles of conceptions discusses the hierarchy of species. It first restates that substance is the genus of genera. Then it explains that just as genera ascend, so do relative species. Al-Dasuqi clarifies the distinction between relative and real species: relative species can be arranged in a hierarchy (e.g., absolute wheat is a species relative to plant, but a genus relative to Indian wheat), while real species (like Indian wheat) cannot have another real species above it because that would turn it into a genus, which is contradictory. The text also notes that some relative species are 'singular' (like absolute intellect) which have no genus above them and only individuals under them, so they are not arranged. The commentary from al-Sharnubi at the end summarizes that both genera and species have five levels, categorized into most specific, highest, and intermediate. This reinforces the lesson's focus on the hierarchical order of universals.

نوع إضافي
نوع حقيقي
الجنس السافل
النوع المفرد

The Five Universals

Relative Species and Species of Species
Author defines descending relative species and identifies the lowest species as species of species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define relative species (النوع الإضافي) and distinguish it from real species, and identify the 'species of species' (نوع الأنواع) in any hierarchical classification.

Core Idea
Species can be relative: what is a species of a higher genus can be a genus for lower species.

In the five universals, species and genus are relative concepts, but real species cannot be nested. This passage clarifies the arrangement of relative species and defines the lowest species, called the species of species. Through examples and commentary, you will understand how classification works in a hierarchy from genus to species to individuals.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish Real from Relative Species
Identify that real species (الحقيقية) cannot be arranged hierarchically because if a real species were under another real species, the lower would become a genus—a contradiction. Relative species (الإضافية) can be arranged as a species under a genus, and that same species can serve as a genus for what is below it.
The passage uses 'real species' to mean species that are truly species and never genera.
2
Understand the Single Species (النوع المفرد)
Some relative species have no arrangement—they are 'single species' with no genus above them and only individuals below. The example given is the absolute intellect (العقل المطلق) according to some views. This illustrates that not all species fit a simple chain.
Two views: intellect as genus (with ten intellects as species) or as species (with ten intellects as individuals).
3
Recognize the Descending Arrangement
Relative species can be arranged descendingly from higher (more general) to lower (more specific). The lowest in the chain is the 'species of species' (نوع الأنواع). The commentary emphasizes that relative species involve ordering by specificity.
Example given: body → growing body → animal → man. Man is the species of species.
4
Identify the Species of Species in Given Examples
In the example, body is a relative species: above it is substance (genus), below it are species like growing body, animal, man. Man is the lowest relative species because under it are only individual humans. This is the species of species.
The passage notes that body is also a genus when predicated of its own species, showing its dual role.
5
Apply to New Hierarchies
Given a classification tree (e.g., substance → body → growing body → plant → wheat), identify which are relative species and which is the species of species. The species of species is always the one with no lower relative species under it.
The key is to check if the term can be predicated of lower species or only of individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : كَذَلِكَ الْأَنْوَاعُ الْإِضَافِيَّةُ قَدْ تَتَرَتَّبُ احترزَ بالإضافية عن الحقيقية، فإِنَّهُ يستحيلُ ترتبها بحيث يكون نوع حقيقي تحت نوع آخر حقيقي؛ لأنَّها لو ترتَّبَتْ؛ لَكَانَ النَّوعُ الحقيقي جنساً، وهو مُحالٌ ; لِمَا يلزم عليه من كون الأفرادِ الَّتي يُقالُ عليها مُتَّفقةُ الحقيقة؛ مُختَلِفَتَها وهو تناقض وأتى بـ «قَدْ) في قوله : (قد تتر ، : تتربَّبُ)؛ لأنَّ بعضَ الأنواع الإضافيَّةِ ليس فيها ترتيب كما في النوع المفرد، وهو ما ليس فوقَهُ جنس، وتحتَهُ أفراد مُتَّفقةُ الحقيقة، وذلكَ كَالعقل المطلق؛ بناءً على أنَّ العطار (قَوْلُهُ : الإِضَافِيَّةُ) وأمَّا الحقيقيَّةُ؛ فَيستحيلُ تَرتُبهَا، وإلا؛ لَكَانَ النَّوعُ الحقيقي جنساً، وهو باطل. فلاندراج غيره تحته ولاندراجه هو تحت الجوهر وهو جنس الأجناس، وبما ذكرنا من الأمثلة تعلم أن للأجناس خمس مراتب مرتبة ترتيباً تصاعدياً، وللأنواع خمساً مرتبة ترتيباً تنازلياً، وأن الخمسة الأوّل ثلاثة أقسام، الأوّل أخصها وهو مطلق قمح وذرة ... إلخ، والثَّاني أعلاها وهو الجوهر، والثَّالث متوسط وهو ثلاثة: النبات والجسم النامي والجسم، وأن الخمسة الأخر ثلاثة أقسام أيضاً، الأول: أعلى الأنواع وهو جسم، والثاني : أخصها وهو القمح الهندي والذرة العويجة... إلخ، والثالث: متوسط بينهما ثلاثة أيضاً مطلق قمح أو ذرة ... إلخ، والنبات والجسم النامي. ا.هـ. الشَّرنوبي . وهو  قد تَتَرَتَّبُ مُتَنَازِلَةٌ) بأن يكون نوع تحته نوع، وهكذا (إِلَى) النوع (الشافِلِ). (وَيُسَمَّى) ذلكَ النَّوعُ السَّافِلُ نَوْعَ الأَنْوَاعِ) كالجسم مثلاً، فإنَّه نوع إضافي تحته نوع، وهو الجسمُ النَّامي، وتحته الحيوان، وتحته الإنسان، فالإنسان نوع الأنواع. الدسوقي الجوهر غيرُ جنس له ؛ لأنَّ العقولَ العَشَرةَ المندرجة تحته أفراد لهُ مُتَّفقةُ الحقيقة، واختلافها إنَّما هو بالخواص والعوارض ; كاختلافِ أفراد الإنسان. والحاصل : أنَّ العقل؛ قِيلَ : إِنَّهُ جنس ; مُختلفةٌ أنواعه بالفصولِ، وَقِيلَ : إِنَّهُ نوع مختلفةٌ أفراده بالخواص، فَعَلَى الأَوَّلِ : يكونُ جنساً منفرداً؛ لكونه ليس فوقَهُ جنس، وتحته أنواع حقيقيَّة، وهي العقول العشرة، وعلى الثَّاني : يكون نوعاً مفرداً ; لأنَّه ليس فوقه جنس وتحته أفراد، وهي العقول العشرة؛ بناءً على أنَّ رأيَ الحكماء مِنْ إثباتها وإثبات الجواهر المجرَّدةِ مِنَ المواد الجسميَّة، وأنَّ الجوهر ليس جنساً لِمَا تحتَهُ؛ لأنَّهُ حينَئِذٍ مَقولُ بالتَّشكيكِ على المجردات وغيرِها، وشرط الجنس التَّواطقُ كَمَا مرَّ ، وأمَّا على القول بعدم المجرَّداتِ؛ فالجوهر جنسٌ لِمَا تحته، لأنَّهُ مَقول عليه بالتَّواطؤ . (قَوْلُهُ : مُتَنَازِلَةٌ) أي : في الخصوصيَّة، منتهية إلى السافل. (قَوْلُهُ: وَيُسَمَّى نَوْعَ الْأَنْوَاعِ لأنَّ النَّوعِيَّةَ الإضافية لا يجري الترتيب فيها إلا باعتبار الخصوص، فأخصُّ الكلِّ ; نوع الكلِّ، وأعمُّهَا; سافل(۱)، وَمَا بينَهُمَا ; متوسط . (قَوْلُهُ : كَالْجِسْمِ مَثَلاً ; فَإِنَّهُ نَوْعٌ إِضَافِيٌّ أي : لأنَّ فوقه الجنس، وهو جوهر; لأَنَّهُ يصدقُ على الجسم، والسّطح والخط، وعلى الجوهر الفرد أيضاً عند المتكلّمين ، والجسم وإنْ كانَ نوعاً بالإضافة للجوهر ; هو جنس باعتبار مقوليَّتِهِ على العطار (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : وأعمها سافل الصواب وأعمّها أعلاها كما لا يخفى . ا . هـ . الشرنوبي .
His saying: Likewise, relative species may be arranged. He restricted to 'relative' to exclude real [species], for it is impossible for them to be arranged such that a real species is under another real species; because if they were arranged, the real species would be a genus, which is impossible due to the necessary consequence that the individuals predicated of them are identical in essence yet different, which is a contradiction. And he used 'may' in his saying 'may be arranged' because some relative species have no arrangement, like the single species, which is that above which there is no genus and under which there are individuals identical in essence, such as the absolute intellect, according to... al-Attar: His saying: 'relative' — as for real [species], their arrangement is impossible, otherwise the real species would be a genus, which is false. For other things are included under it, and it is included under substance, which is the genus of genera. From the examples we have mentioned, you learn that genera have five ranks ascending, and species have five ranks descending, and that the five [genera] are three sections: the most specific of them is absolute wheat, corn, etc., the highest of them is substance, and the middle is three: plant, growing body, and body; and that the five [species] are also three sections: the highest of species is body, the most specific of them is Indian wheat and twisted corn, etc., and the middle between them is three also: absolute wheat or corn, etc., and plant and growing body. End of al-Sharnubi's commentary. And it is [PAGE_366] may be arranged descendingly, such that there is a species under a species, and so on up to the lowest species. And that lowest species is called the species of species, like body for example, for it is a relative species under which is a species, which is the growing body, under which is animal, under which is man, so man is the species of species. al-Dasuqi: Substance is not a genus for it [the intellect] because the ten intellects included under it are individuals of it identical in essence, their difference being only by properties and accidents, like the difference among individuals of man. The conclusion: regarding the intellect, it is said that it is a genus whose species differ by differentia, and it is said that it is a species whose individuals differ by properties. On the first view, it is a unique genus, because there is no genus above it and under it are real species, namely the ten intellects. On the second view, it is a single species, because there is no genus above it and under it are individuals, namely the ten intellects, based on the philosophers' opinion in establishing them and establishing incorporeal substances, and that substance is not a genus for what is under it, because then it would be predicated equivocally on incorporeal and corporeal things, and the condition of genus is univocality, as has passed. But on the view that there are no incorporeal substances, substance is a genus for what is under it, because it is predicated univocally. His saying: 'descendingly' i.e., in specificity, ending at the lowest. His saying: 'and it is called species of species' because relative species-ness only involves arrangement according to specificity, so the most specific of all is the species of all, and the most general of them is the lowest, and what is between them is intermediate. His saying: 'like body for example, for it is a relative species' i.e., because above it is a genus, which is substance; because it is true of body, surface, line, and also the indivisible substance according to the theologians. And body, even if it is a species relative to substance, is a genus by virtue of being predicated on... al-Attar (1) His saying: 'and the most general of them is lowest' — the correct is 'and the most general of them is highest' as is not hidden. End of al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage focuses on relative species (الأنواع الإضافية) and defines the concept of 'species of species' (نوع الأنواع). The author begins by noting that relative species can be arranged in descending order, unlike real species (الحقيقية) which cannot be arranged hierarchically because if a real species were under another real species, the lower would become a genus, which is contradictory. The commentary explains that some relative species, like the 'single species' (النوع المفرد), have no arrangement—an example is the absolute intellect (العقل المطلق) on a certain view. Al-Dasuqi then discusses whether intellect is a genus or a species, depending on whether one accepts incorporeal substances. On the view that intellect is a genus, it is a unique genus (جنس منفرد) with no genus above it; on the view that it is a species, it is a single species (نوع مفرد). This depends on whether substance is predicated univocally or equivocally. The passage then provides the main definition: relative species descend until the lowest, which is called species of species. The example given is body (جسم) as a relative species under which is growing body, then animal, then man—so man is the species of species. The commentary further clarifies that the term 'species of species' is used because relative species are ordered by specificity: the most specific is the species of all, the most general is the lowest (but a marginal correction notes that 'most general' should be 'highest'). Also, body is explained to be a relative species because above it is substance, and it is a genus when predicated of its own species.

نوع الأنواع
نوع مفرد

A relative species can be a genus for what is below it; the lowest relative species, under which there are only individuals, is called the species of species.

The Descending Species and Type of Types
Author defines descending chain of species and names the lowest 'type of types'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the lowest species in a descending chain and explain the meaning of 'type of types'.

Core Idea
The 'type of types' is not the highest genus but the most specific species.

In the classification of universals, species can be arranged in a descending hierarchy where each species falls under a higher one. The lowest species in this chain, which has no species beneath it, is given a special name: 'type of types'. This passage explains that concept with a clear example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'descending species' (متنازلة) and 'type of types' (نوع الأنواع) in the Arabic text. Note that the passage defines the latter as the lowest species (السافل).
2
Understand the hierarchy
The passage describes a chain: a species is under another species, and so on, until reaching the lowest. Each intermediate species is relative (نوع إضافي) because it can be seen as a genus to what is below.
3
Trace the example
The example is: body (جسم) → growing body (جسم نامي) → animal (حيوان) → human (إنسان). Verify that each step is a species under the previous one.
4
Identify the type of types
In the chain, human is the lowest species because no species is under it. Therefore, human is the 'type of types' (نوع الأنواع) in this example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَدْ تَتَرَتَّبُ مُتَنَازِلَةٌ) بأن يكون نوع تحته نوع، وهكذا (إِلَى) النوع (الشافِلِ). (وَيُسَمَّى) ذلكَ النَّوعُ السَّافِلُ نَوْعَ الأَنْوَاعِ) كالجسم مثلاً، فإنَّه نوع إضافي تحته نوع، وهو الجسمُ النَّامي، وتحته الحيوان، وتحته الإنسان، فالإنسان نوع الأنواع.
(They may be arranged descendingly) such that a species is under a species, and so on (until) the (lowest) species. (And that lowest species is called) the type of types. For example, body: it is a relative species under which is a species, namely growing body, and under it is animal, and under it is human, so human is the type of types.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of a descending chain of species, where each species is subordinate to a higher one until reaching the lowest species, which is termed 'type of types' (naw' al-anwa'). The author illustrates this with the example of 'body' (jism). Body is a relative species (naw' idafi) because it is under the genus 'substance' (jawhar) and above other species. Under body is 'growing body' (al-jism al-nami), under which is 'animal', under which is 'human'. Thus human is the lowest species in this chain, making it the type of types. This example demonstrates how the hierarchy of species descends from more general to more specific until reaching a species that has no species below it.

نوع إضافي
متنازلة

Key Takeaway
The lowest species in a descending chain is called 'type of types'; it has no species under it.

5 min
Ordering of Genera and Species: Identifying Intermediates
Author explains the descending order of species and ascending order of genera, with intermediate levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the highest genus, lowest species, and intermediates in a given hierarchy, and avoid confusing the relative status of a term as genus or species across different categories.

Core Idea
Genera ascend, species descend; the same term can be both genus and species at different levels.

In this passage, the commentator explains the fundamental ordering of genera and species—a key concept in logic that underlies definitions and classifications. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for correctly applying the five universals, especially when a term serves as both a genus and a species depending on its level. We will walk through the principles and a common pitfall that the commentator carefully corrects.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two directions
Note that species are arranged in descending order (from higher to lower), while genera are arranged in ascending order (from lower to higher). This is a deliberate contrast: species go down, genera go up.
2
Identify the extremes
The highest genus (jins al-ajnas) has no genus above it, e.g., substance. The lowest species (naw' al-anwa') has no species below it, e.g., human. Locate these in the text.
3
Locate intermediates
Terms that are neither the highest nor lowest in their respective hierarchy are intermediates. For example, body is an intermediate genus (above it is substance, below it is animal). But be careful: the author's wording might seem to mix categories.
4
Understand the commentator's correction
The commentator warns that what is between genera and what is between species must be considered separately. Otherwise, body would be wrongly called an intermediate species (it is actually the highest species) and animal an intermediate genus (it is actually the lowest genus).
This is the key insight to avoid confusion.
5
Apply to the example
In the category of substance: highest genus = substance, lowest species = human, intermediate genus = body (since body is above animal and below substance), intermediate species = animal (since animal is above human and below body). Note that animal is a species at a higher level and a genus at a lower level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التَّصورات وإنَّما اعتُبِرَتِ الأنواع بحسَب التَّنازُلِ ؛ لأنّا إذا فرضْنَا شيئاً وفرضْنَا نوعه يكون ذلكَ النَّوعُ تحتَهُ ، ثمَّ إذا فرضنا لذلك النوع نوعاً آخر؛ يكون تحت ذلك النوع. فلهذا كان ترتُبُ الأنواع على سبيل التنازل، ويسمَّى السَّافلُ منها نوع الأنواع، أما إذا فرضنا شيئاً، وفرضنا له جنساً؛ يكون جنسه فوقه، ثمَّ إذا فرضنا له جنساً، يكون فوق ذلك الجنس، وهَلُمَّ جرا . فَلِهذا كان ترتُبُ الأجناس على سبيل التصاعد، ويُسمى العالي منها جنس الأجناس. (وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا)؛ أي: ما بينَ السَّافل والعالي من الأجناس والأنواع ( مُتَوَسّطَاتٌ)؛ لأنَّها ليست عاليةً ولا سافلة، بل متوسطة بينهما. فالمتوسط في مراتب الأجناس : هو الجسم النامي . (قَوْلُهُ : وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا مُتَوَسّطَاتٌ الأوْلَى أنْ تُراعي الأنواع على حِدَة، والأجناس على حِدَة؛ كأن نقول أعلى الأنواع جسم، وأسفلها إنسان، وكذا الأجناس : أعلاها الجوهر، وأسفلها حيوان، والمتوسط ما بينَهُما . وظاهر كلام المصنّف يقتضي أنَّ جسماً يُقال له: جنس متوسط، ونوع متوسط، وهو مُسلَّمْ في الأَوَّلِ دونَ الثَّاني ؛ لِكونِهِ أعلى الأنواع، ويقتضي أنَّ حيواناً نوعٌ متوسط، وجنس متوسط، وهو مُسلَّم في الأوَّلِ دونَ الثَّاني؛ لأنَّهُ أَسفلُ الأجناس . وَوجه الاقتضاء المذكور : أنَّ الأعلى مِنَ الأنواع الجوهر، وأسفلها الإنسان، وهذا يقتضي أنَّ ما بينهما يُقال لهُ: جنسٌ مُتوسط، ونوع متوسط، فتدبَّر، ولكنَّ المرادَ مِنَ المصنّف ظاهر . (قَوْلُهُ : فَالْمُتَوَسَطُ فِي مَرَاتِبِ الْأَجْنَاسِ : هُوَ الْجِسْمُ النَّامِي) أي : لأنَّ فوقَهُ جنس ؛ هو مطلق جسم، وتحت جنس، وهو حيوان . (قَوْلُهُ : هُوَ الْجِسْمُ النَّامِي لَا يَحْفَى أَنَّ الكلام في المعاني المفردة، والجسم النَّامي مُركَّب، والجواب : أنَّ المقصودَ حَصْرُ الأجزاء المفردة في الجنس والفصل؛ لَا حَصْرُ الجنسِ والفصل في الأجزاء المفردة، قال عبد الحكيم: والحقُّ أَنَّهُ لا وجهَ لِجَعْل الجنسيَّةِ والفصلية دائرةً على الألفاظ. (قَوْلُهُ : ووجه الاقتضاء ... إلخ) توضيحه أن المصنف لم يبين السافل من الأجناس ولا الأعلى من الأنواع وبين العالي من الأجناس كالجوهر والسافل من الأنواع كالإنسان، ثم قال : وما بينهما متوسطات، فيدخل فيه الحيوان فيكون جنساً متوسطاً ويدخل فيه الجسم فيكون نوعاً متوسطاً، وهو باطل والجواب : أن مراده بما بينهما أي: بينهما لكل منهما على حدة.
Chapter: On the Principles of Concepts. Indeed, species are considered in descending order, because if we assume a thing and assume its species, that species is below it. Then, if we assume for that species another species, it is below that species. Thus, the arrangement of species is in descending order, and the lowest of them is called 'species of species'. As for if we assume a thing and assume a genus for it, its genus is above it. Then, if we assume a genus for that genus, it is above that genus, and so forth. Thus, the arrangement of genera is in ascending order, and the highest of them is called 'genus of genera'. (And what is between them) i.e., what is between the lowest and the highest of genera and species, (are intermediates) because they are neither high nor low, but mediate between them. The intermediate among the ranks of genera is the growing body (jism namī). (His statement: 'And what is between them are intermediates') It would be better to consider species separately and genera separately; such that we say: the highest of species is body, and the lowest is human, and likewise for genera: the highest is substance, and the lowest is animal, and the intermediate is what is between them. The apparent wording of the author implies that body is called an intermediate genus and an intermediate species. The former is accepted but not the latter, because it is the highest species. And it implies that animal is an intermediate species and an intermediate genus. The former is accepted but not the latter, because it is the lowest genus. The reason for this implication is that the highest of species is substance and the lowest is human, and this implies that what is between them is called an intermediate genus and an intermediate species. So reflect, but the intent of the author is clear. (His statement: 'The intermediate among the ranks of genera is the growing body') i.e., because above it is a genus, which is absolute body, and below it is a genus, which is animal. (His statement: 'It is the growing body') It is not hidden that the discussion is about simple meanings, and the growing body is composite. The answer: The intent is to confine simple parts to genus and differentia, not to confine genus and differentia to simple parts. ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm said: The truth is that there is no justification for making genus-ness and differentia-ness depend on words. (His statement: 'The reason for the implication...') Its clarification: The author did not specify the lowest of genera nor the highest of species, and he mentioned the highest of genera like substance and the lowest of species like human. Then he said 'and what is between them are intermediates', so animal would be included and thus be an intermediate genus, and body would be included and thus be an intermediate species, which is false. The answer: His intent by 'what is between them' is between them for each [category] separately.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section from a commentary on the principles of concepts in logic. It explains the ordering of genera and species. The author begins by stating that species are considered in descending order: if you assume a thing and then its species, that species is below it; if you further assume a species of that species, it is lower still, and so on. Hence, the arrangement of species is descending, and the lowest species (with no species below it) is called 'species of species' (naw' al-anwa'). Conversely, genera are considered in ascending order: if you assume a thing and then its genus, that genus is above it; if you assume a genus of that genus, it is above that, and so on. Hence, the arrangement of genera is ascending, and the highest genus (with no genus above it) is called 'genus of genera' (jins al-ajnas). Between the highest and lowest there are intermediate levels, which are both genera and species relative to different levels. The commentator then gives examples: in genera, the intermediate is the growing body (jism namī) because above it is absolute body (a higher genus) and below it is animal (a lower genus). He also provides a refinement: the apparent wording of the matn might lead one to think that body is an intermediate species (which is false because body is the highest species) and that animal is an intermediate genus (which is false because animal is the lowest genus). The correct understanding is that 'between them' refers to between the highest and lowest within each category separately, not mixing the two. Finally, a comment addresses an objection: the discussion is about simple concepts, but 'growing body' is composite. The response is that the aim is to confine simple parts to genus and differentia, not the reverse. The lesson guards against confusing the relative status of a term as genus or species across different hierarchies.

نوع
متوسط
جنس الأجناس
نوع الأنواع

Key Takeaway
Genera and species are ordered in opposite directions; intermediate terms are relative and must be identified within their own hierarchy to avoid categorical confusion.

intermediate
8 min
Intermediate Genera and Species: The Hierarchy of Genera vs. Species
Author explains intermediate genera and species between highest and lowest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the hierarchy of genera and the hierarchy of species, and correctly identify intermediate categories in each.

Core Idea
The same term can be an intermediate genus but not an intermediate species—hierarchies of genus and species must be kept separate.

This passage from the Tadhheeb clarifies a subtle point: when we talk about 'intermediate' categories in the five universals, we must carefully separate the ladder of genera from the ladder of species. A term like 'body' sits at different levels depending on which hierarchy we consider. By learning to distinguish these hierarchies, you will avoid a common confusion in logical classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct hierarchies
The author discusses two separate hierarchies: one for genera (highest: substance, lowest: animal) and one for species (highest: body, lowest: man). Notice that the same name ('body', 'animal') can appear in both hierarchies but at different positions.
2
Extract the highest and lowest in each hierarchy
From the passage: highest genus is substance (جوهر), lowest genus is animal (حيوان). Highest species is absolute body (جسم مطلق), lowest species is human (إنسان).
The author explicitly states these extremes.
3
Determine the intermediate in each hierarchy
Intermediate genus: what is between substance and animal? The author gives growing body (جسم نامي) as the intermediate genus. Intermediate species: what is between absolute body and human? The author says it is growing body and animal (but animal is a genus, so the intermediate species are those below absolute body but above human).
Note: animal is a genus, not a species, so its inclusion as intermediate species may be reductive; the author clarifies that the intermediate species are growing body and animal only when considering the species hierarchy?
4
Compare the author's clarification about body and animal
The author warns that body can be called an intermediate genus (accepted) but not an intermediate species (because it is the highest species). Similarly, animal can be called an intermediate species (accepted) but not an intermediate genus (because it is the lowest genus). This shows the need to treat the two hierarchies separately.
The commentator explains that the author's phrase 'what is between them' refers to the extremes of each hierarchy individually, not a combined list.

And what is between them: i.e., what is between the lowest and highest of genera and species are intermediates. Because they are neither high nor low, but intermediate between them. So the intermediate in the ranks of genera is the growing body. (His statement: And what is between them are intermediates) It is better to consider species separately and genera separately; e.g., we say the highest of species is body, and its lowest is man, and likewise genera: their highest is substance, their lowest is animal, and the intermediate is what is between them. The apparent meaning of the author's statement implies that body is called an intermediate genus and an intermediate species, which is accepted in the first but not the second, because it is the highest of species. And it implies that animal is an intermediate species and an intermediate genus, accepted in the first but not the second, because it is the lowest of genera. The reason for the mentioned implication is that the highest of species is substance and its lowest is man, and this implies that what is between them is called an intermediate genus and an intermediate species. Reflect, but the author's intent is clear. (His statement: So the intermediate in the ranks of genera is the growing body) i.e., because above it is a genus, which is absolute body, and below it is a genus, which is animal. (His statement: It is the growing body. It is not hidden that talk is about simple meanings, and the growing body is composite. The answer: The intent is to enumerate the simple parts into genus and differentia, not to enumerate genus and differentia into simple parts.) (His statement: The reason for the implication) Explanation: The author did not specify the lowest of genera nor the highest of species; he specified the highest of genera like substance and the lowest of species like man, then said: what is between them are intermediates. This includes animal as an intermediate genus and includes body as an intermediate species, which is false. The answer: His intent by 'between them' is between the highest and lowest for each separately. (Then he said) The absolute body, and in the ranks of species it is the growing body and animal.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion clarifying the concept of intermediate (mutawassit) categories in the hierarchical classification of genera and species. The author begins by defining 'what is between them' as the entities that are neither the highest nor the lowest. He then uses the example of the growing body (jism nam) as the intermediate genus, with absolute body above it and animal below. The commentary notes a potential confusion: if we consider the entire hierarchy, body could be seen as both an intermediate genus (since it falls under substance) and an intermediate species (since it is above animal). However, the correct view distinguishes between the hierarchy of genera and the hierarchy of species. In the genus hierarchy, the highest is substance, the lowest is animal; in the species hierarchy, the highest is body (absolute body), the lowest is man. The intermediate in each hierarchy is different. The passage guards against conflating the two hierarchies and emphasizes that terms like 'body' and 'animal' can be intermediate in one hierarchy but not the other. The response to a potential objection about the term 'growing body' being composite is that the focus is on enumerating simple parts into genus and differentia, not the reverse. This reflects the logical precision required in the art of definition.

The Differentia as Part of the Essence
Author defines the differentia and distinguishes it from genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the differentia from the genus and identify both as parts of the essence in any definition.

Core Idea
The differentia is the part of the essence that distinguishes one species from another, while the genus is the common part.

In this lesson, we examine the third universal: the differentia (fasl). The author explains that it is part of the essence like the genus, but he intends to show the difference. We will learn how to identify the differentia in a definition and understand its role in distinguishing species.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum in the Arabic: الفصل (fasl). What exactly is being defined?
2
Note that it is a part of the essence
The text states 'وهو وإن كان جزءاً من ماهية الأفراد كالجنس' which means it is a part of the essence of individuals like the genus.
Both genus and differentia are parts of the essence (juz' al-māhiyya).
3
Compare with the genus
Recognize that genus is the broader category common to multiple species, while differentia is what distinguishes one species from another within that genus.
For example, in 'rational animal', 'animal' is the genus and 'rational' is the differentia.
4
Understand the concessive phrase
The phrase 'wa in kana' (even though) signals a concession: both are parts of the essence, but there is a difference.
This construction is common in definitions to highlight a contrast.
5
Apply to a definition
Test any definition: identify the genus and differentia. Ensure the differentia truly distinguishes the species from others in the same genus.
A complete definition (hadd) requires both genus and differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: في مبادئ التصورات (الثَّالِثُ) منَ الكلِّيَّات: (الفَصْلُ)، وهو وإن كان جزءاً من ماهية الأفراد كالجنس، (قَوْلُهُ: وَهُوَ وَإِنْ كَانَ جُزْءاً . . . إِلَخ ) يريدُ أنْ يُبيِّنَ بِذلِكَ الفرق بين الجنس والفصلِ، مَعَ أَنَّ كُلًّا مِنهُما جزء الماهِيَّةِ، ثمَّ إنَّ مثل هذا التركيب كثير الوقوع، وقد يقع؛ لكنْ مَوقِعَ «إلا»، و«إلَّا» و«لكن»؛ ليسَا بِخَبرَيْنِ، بَلْ هُمَا لِلاستدراكَ ، لكنَّهُمَا واقعانِ مَوقِعَ الخبر، وهو مُقدَّرُ حسبما يقتضيه المقام ; قاله المحشّي، وتعقَّبَهُ البعضُ بِمَا قالَهُ عبد الحكيم في حاشية المطوّلِ عند قوله : والهيئة والعرضُ متقاربا المفهوم، إلَّا أنَّ العرضَ يُقَالُ باعتبار عروضه ... إلخ؛ بأنَّ هذه العبارة متعارفةٌ في محاوراتِ العلماء، وتوجيهها أنَّ كلمة «إِلَّا» للاستثناءِ مِنْ مُقدَّرٍ ; تَقديرُهُ : لا فرقَ بينَهُما إلا بهذا الاعتبار، وليست استدراكية كما وهم ا.هـ. وفيهِ أنَّهُ قد نقل عبارةَ المطوَّلِ على غيرِ مَا هِيَ عليه، فإنَّ عبارتهُ كَمَا نقلْنَا، وقال: هو
Chapter: On the principles of conceptualization. (The third) of the universals: (the differentia/fasl). And it, even though it is a part of the essence of individuals like the genus. (His statement: And it, even though it is a part ... etc.) He intends to show thereby the difference between genus and differentia, despite the fact that both are part of the essence. Then such a construction occurs frequently, and it may occur, but the position of 'except', 'except', and 'but' are not predicates; rather they are for istidrak (rectification/exception). However, they occur in the position of the predicate, which is estimated according to what the context requires, as the commentator said. And some criticized him with what Abd al-Hakim said in the margin of al-Mutawwal concerning his statement: 'The form and the accident are similar in concept, except that the accident is said with consideration of its occurrence ... etc.'; that this expression is conventional in scholarly discourses, and its interpretation is that the word 'except' is for an exception from something estimated, the estimation being: 'There is no difference between them except by this consideration,' and it is not an istidrak as someone imagined. And therein is that he transmitted the wording of al-Mutawwal not as it is, for his wording is as we transmitted, and he said: it is
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on the third universal, the differentia (fasl). The author begins by stating that the differentia is a part of the essence of individuals, just like the genus, but he intends to highlight the difference. The commentary delves into a linguistic-grammatical discussion about the construction 'wa in kana...' which is a concessive clause ('even though...'). The discussion revolves around how to interpret the words 'illa' and 'lakin' in such contexts: are they predicates or are they for istidrak (a type of rhetorical correction)? The commentator argues that they are not predicates but occur in the place of a predicate, and the actual predicate is estimated. He then critiques a view attributed to Abd al-Hakim, who said that such expressions are conventional and that 'illa' is for exception from an estimated missing phrase (e.g., 'there is no difference between them except...'). The passage thus refines the understanding of how to parse such concessive statements in logical texts, showing the nuance between grammar and logic. The deeper point is that the differentia, while being a part of the essence like genus, is distinguished by being the part that differentiates the species, whereas the genus is the broader common part. This textual operation is a critical refinement (hāshiyah) because it corrects a potential misunderstanding of the grammatical role of 'illa' and 'lakin' in such contexts.

جزء الماهية
استدراك

Parsing Concessive Statements in Definitions
Author defines the differentia and distinguishes it from genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the function of 'illa' and 'lakin' in concessive definitional statements and distinguish between istidrak and exception.

Core Idea
The word 'illa' in definitional statements is often an exception from an estimated phrase, not a predicate or istidrak.

In definitions, scholars often use phrases like 'X is Y except that...' This lesson unpacks the logical and grammatical function of such phrases, drawing on the commentary's analysis of 'illa' and 'lakin' in the definition of the differentia. We will learn how to parse these constructions correctly.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the concessive construction
Identify the phrase 'wa in kana ... illa ...' or similar. This structure signals a concession: the first part acknowledges a commonality, the second part introduces a distinction.
Example from the passage: 'وهو وإن كان جزءاً من ماهية الأفراد كالجنس' (even though it is a part of the essence like the genus).
2
Understand the function of 'illa' and 'lakin'
According to the commentator, words like 'illa' and 'lakin' in such contexts are not predicates but are used for istidrak (rectification) or for exception from an estimated phrase.
The debate is whether 'illa' is istidrakiyya or istithnā'iyya.
3
Analyze the estimated missing phrase
The proposed estimation is: 'لا فرق بينهما إلا بهذا الاعتبار' (there is no difference between them except by this consideration). This shows that 'illa' introduces an exception from a negative statement.
This interpretation avoids the need for 'illa' to be a predicate.
4
Contrast with the istidrak view
Some thought 'illa' is for istidrak (like 'but' in English), but the commentator argues it is for exception. This affects how we understand the logical force of the statement.
In 'illa' for exception, the preceding negative is implied, which is different from a direct contrast.
5
Apply to a definition in an Islamic science
Take a definition like 'The evidence is the scriptural proofs, except that consensus is a secondary evidence.' Identify the estimated missing clause and determine if 'except' is for exception or istidrak.
Practice parsing such statements to enhance precision in reading logical definitions.

Close and Distant Differentia (الفصل القريب والبعيد)
Defines الفصل and its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between close and distant differentia and identify which type is used in a given definition from the Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Differentia is the defining part of a species – but not all differentiae are unique.

The third universal, الفصل (differentia), plays a crucial role in definition. Like genus, it is part of the essence of a species, but unlike genus, it does not capture the complete commonality with another species. Understanding the two types—close and distant—is essential for constructing precise definitions in Islamic disciplines like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate 'الفصل' in the Arabic passage and note that it is part of the essence (ماهية) of individuals, just like genus, but differs in function.
2
Distinguish genus and differentia
The author states that genus is 'تمام المشترك' (the complete commonality) between a species and another, while differentia is not the complete commonality. This is the key difference.
3
Understand the two types of differentia
Close differentia (الفصل القريب) has no commonality at all with other species (e.g., ناطق for man). Distant differentia (الفصل البعيد) is shared with another species but not as the full commonality (e.g., نامي shared by man, horse, tree).
4
Apply the distinction to examples
From the passage: ناطق is close differentia because only man is rational. نامي and حساس are distant because they are shared but not the complete commonality between man and horse (the complete commonality is حيوان).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثَّالِثُ) منَ الكلِّيَّات: (الفَصْلُ)، وهو وإن كان جزءاً من ماهية الأفراد كالجنس، إلا أنه ليس تمام المشترك بين الماهية ونوع آخر. وحاصل ما ذكر من الفرق بين الجنس والفصل: أن الجنس هو ما كان تمام المشترك بين الماهية ونوع آخر، وأن الفصل ما لا يكون تمام المشترك بين الماهية ونوع آخر، وذلك صادق بأن لا يقع فيه اشتراك أصلاً، وهو الفصل القريب ناطق، أو يقع فيه اشتراك بين الماهية ونوع آخر، ولكنه لا يكون تمام المشترك بينهما كالنامي، فإنه وقع الاشتراك فيه بين الإنسان والفرس، ولكنه ليس تمام المشترك بينهما، وإنما تمام المشترك بينهما الحيوان وحينئذ؛ فهو إنما يميزه عن الحجر، ولا يميزه عن الفرس ولا عن الشجر، وهذا هو الفصل البعيد. وكذلك حساس؛ فإنه وقع فيه الاشتراك بين الإنسان والفرس، لكنه ليس تمام المشترك بينهما، بل تمام المشترك بينهما الحيوان، وحينئذ؛ فحساس إنما يميز الإنسان عن الحجر وعن الشجر؛ لا عن الفرس، فهو فصل بعيد، فافهم.
The third of the universals: the differentia. And although it is part of the essence of individuals like the genus, it is not the complete commonality between the essence and another type. The gist of what was mentioned of the difference between genus and differentia: that genus is what is the complete commonality between the essence and another type, and that differentia is what is not the complete commonality between the essence and another type. That applies either to no commonality occurring at all, which is the close differentia, e.g., rational; or commonality occurs between the essence and another type but it is not the complete commonality between them, like 'growing', for commonality in it occurs between man and horse, but it is not the complete commonality between them; rather the complete commonality between them is animal. Then it distinguishes man from stone, but does not distinguish him from horse or tree. This is the distant differentia. Likewise 'sensitive': commonality occurs in it between man and horse, but it is not the complete commonality between them; rather the complete commonality between them is animal. Then 'sensitive' distinguishes man from stone and tree, but not from horse. So it is a distant differentia. Understand.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage defines the third universal (الفصل) and explains its distinction from genus (الجنس). Both genus and differentia are parts of the essence (ماهية) of individuals, but they differ in function: genus is the complete common element shared between a species and another species (e.g., animal is shared between man and horse), while differentia is not the complete common element. The passage then classifies differentia into close (قريب) and distant (بعيد). Close differentia (e.g., ناطق – rational) is not shared with any other species at all, making it uniquely distinctive. Distant differentia (e.g., نامي – growing, or حساس – sensitive) is shared with other species but is not the full commonality. For example, 'growing' is shared by man, horse, and tree, but the full commonality between man and horse is 'animal'. Thus 'growing' distinguishes man from stone (which does not grow) but not from horse or tree. 'Sensitive' similarly distinguishes man from stone and tree but not from horse, because horse is also sensitive. This shows that distant differentia alone is insufficient to fully define a species.

تمام المشترك
الفصل القريب
الفصل البعيد

Key Takeaway
The differentia is the part of the essence that distinguishes a species, and it can be either close (unique) or distant (shared but not fully).

The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Distinction between Genus and Differentia via Complete Commonality
Dasuqi explains the distinction between genus and differentia using the criterion of complete commonality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia for any species using the criterion of complete commonality and classify differentiae as proximate or distant.

Core Idea
Genus is the complete commonality; differentia is not. This distinction is the key to precise definitions in Islamic logic.

The five universals are the building blocks of definitions. Among them, genus and differentia are often confused. Al-Dasūqī clarifies using the criterion of 'complete commonality' (tamām al-mushtarak). Understanding this criterion allows you to see why 'rational' is a differentia but 'sentient' is not the complete commonality, and why 'animal' is a genus. This passage walks you through the distinction with concrete examples from creation and then applies it to logical theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion
The passage states that genus is the complete commonality (tamām al-mushtarak) between an essence and another species, while differentia is not. Understand this as the central distinguishing feature.
2
Apply the criterion to examples
For man and horse, examine predicates: 'body', 'growing body', 'sentient body', 'animal'. The most specific shared predicate is 'animal'—that is the genus. 'Sentient' and 'growing' are shared but not the complete commonality, so they are not genera.
The passage shows that 'sentient' is a distant differentia because it distinguishes man from stone but not from horse.
3
Distinguish proximate vs. distant differentia
Proximate differentia has no commonality with another species (e.g., 'rational' for man). Distant differentia has some commonality but not as the complete commonality (e.g., 'sentient' for man shares with horse but the complete commonality is 'animal').
This distinction helps in evaluating definitions: a definition using a distant differentia is less precise.
4
Test with a new example
Consider the species 'horse' and 'donkey'. Their complete commonality is 'equine'? Actually, it would be 'animal' again, or more specifically 'mammal'? In logical terms, the most specific commonality is 'equine'. But to practice, use the same reasoning: find the most specific shared predicate, that is the genus; any predicate less specific that still distinguishes partially is a differentia.
You can also test with a fiqh example: define 'salah' by its genus (e.g., 'worship') and differentia (e.g., 'specific actions').
